The City of Hampton, Virginia $10,725,000 General Obligation Public Improvement Bonds (Environmental Impact Bonds) 
(Green Bonds), Series 2020A (Tax-Exempt) (the “Series 2020 A Bonds”) and $16,895,000 General Obligation Public Improvement 
Refunding Bonds, Series 2020B (Federally Taxable) (the “Serie s 2020B Bonds” and, together with the Series 2020A Bonds, the 
“Bonds”) will be issued in fully registered form without coupons.  The Bonds will be registered in the name of Cede & Co., as nominee 
of The Depository Trust Company, New York, New York (“DTC”).  DTC will act as securities depository for the Bonds.  So long as 
Cede & Co. is the registered owner of the Bonds, as nominee for DT C, principal and interest shall be payable to Cede & Co., as 
nominee for DTC, which will, in turn, remit such principal and inte rest to the DTC participants for subsequent disbursement to the 
beneficial owners of the Bonds.  Individual purchases of the Bonds will be made in book-entry form only in denominations of $5,000 
or multiples thereof.  Beneficial owners of the Bonds will not receive physical delivery of Bond certificates. 
The Bonds mature on September 1 in each of the years and amounts set forth herein.  Interest on the Bonds is payable at the 
rates specified herein on each March 1 and September 1, commencing March 1, 2021.  Interest on the Bonds will be calculated on the 
basis of a 30-day month and a 360-day year. 
The Bonds are general obligations of the City to the payment of which its full faith and credit are pledged.  The City Council 
of the City (the “City Council”) is authorized and required by  law, unless other funds are lawfully available and appropriated for 
timely payment of the Bonds, to levy and collect at the same time and in the same manner as other taxes of the City are assesse d, 
levied and collected an ad valorem tax, over and above all other taxes authorized or limited by law, unlimited as to rate or am ount, 
upon all property within the City subject to taxation by the City sufficient to pay the principal of and premium, if any, and interest on 
the Bonds. 
The Series 2020A Bonds are subject to optional redemption prior to  their stated maturities as more fully described herein.  
The Series 2020B Bonds may not be redeemed prior to maturity. 
The Bonds are offered for delivery when, as, and if issued, subject to the legal opinion of Bond Counsel, Kutak Rock 
LLP, Richmond, Virginia.  Certain legal matters will be passed upon for the City by Cheran Cordell Ivery, Esquire, City 
Attorney,  and for the underwriters by McGuireWoods LLP, Richmo nd, Virginia.  It is expected that the Bonds will be 
available for delivery through the facilities of DTC on or about December 2, 2020.

PART I 
 
INTRODUCTION 
General 
The purpose of this Official Statement (which includes the cover page and appendices hereto) is to 
furnish information in connection with the sale by the City of Hampton (herein called the “City” or 
“Hampton”) in the Commonwealth of Virginia (herei n called the “Commonwealth” or “State”) of its 
$10,725,000 General Obligation Publ ic Improvement Bonds (Environm ental Impact Bonds) (Green 
Bonds), Series 2020A (Tax-Exempt) (the “Series 2020A  Bonds”) and its $16,895,000 General Obligation 
Public Improvement Refunding Bonds, Series 2020B (Federally Taxable) (the “Series 2020B Bonds” and, 
together with the Series 2020A Bonds, the “Bonds”).  The Bonds will be general obligations of the City, to 
the payment of which the full faith and credit of the City are irrevocably pledged.  This Official Statement 
has been approved and authorized by the City for use in connection with the sale of the Bonds.  Financial 
and other information contained in this Official Statement have been prepared by the City from its records, 
except where other sources are noted.  The information is not intended to indicate future or continuing 
trends in the financial or economic trends of the City.  This Official Statement should be read in its entirety. 
This Official Statement contains forecasts, proj ections and estimates that are based on current 
expectations but are not intended as representations of f act or guarantees of results.  If and when included 
in this Official Statement, th e words “expects,” “forecasts,” “pro jects,” “intends,” “anticipates,” 
“estimates,” and analogous expressions are intended to identify forward-looking statements as defined in 
the Securities Act of 1933, as amended, and any such statements inherently are subject to a variety of risks 
and uncertainties, which could cause actual results to differ materially from those contemplated in such 
forward-looking statements.  These forward looking statem ents speak only as of the date of this Official 
Statement.  The City disclaims any obligation or unde rtaking to release publicly any updates or revisions 
to any forward-looking statement contained herein to re flect any change in the City’s expectations with 
regard thereto or any change in events, conditions or circumstances on which any such statement is based. 
References to web site addresses presented in this Official Statement are for informational purposes 
only and may be in the form of a hyperlink solely for the reader's convenience.  Unless specified otherwise, 
such web sites and the information or links containe d therein are not incorporated into, and are not part 
of, this final official statement for purposes of, and as that term is defined in, SEC rule 15c2-12.   
There follows in this Official Statement a brie f description of the Bonds and a description of the

The City 
The City, settled in 1610 and the oldest Englis h-speaking community in continuous existence in 
the United States, is rapidly emerging as one of the Commonwealth of Virginia’s leading high technology 
centers.  Located on the extreme southeasterly tip of th e lower Peninsula of Virgin ia, the City is bounded 
on the west by the City of Newport News, on the no rth by York County and the City of Poquoson, on the 
east by the Chesapeake Bay, and on the south by the harbor of Hampton Roads.  Estimated population in 
Hampton was 135,629 in 2019. 
DESCRIPTION OF THE BONDS 
Authorization and Purpose 
 The Bonds were authorized to be issued by a resolution duly adopted by the City Council on 
September 23, 2020 (the “Resolution”) pursuant to the Public Finance Act of 1991, the same being 
Chapter 26, Title 15.2, Code of Virginia of 1950, as amended (the “Act”).   
The Series 2020A Bonds are being issued to fu nd certain stormwater management projects 
consisting primarily of pilot projects designed to mitigate the impact of stormwater-induced flooding 
through the control, redirection, treatment and storag e of stormwater using environmentally advantageous 
and sustainable methods. Proceeds of the Series 2020A Bonds will also be used to fund issuance costs.   
The Series 2020B Bonds are being issued to refund in advance of their stated maturities portions 
of the City’s general obligation bonds  as more particularly described in Appendix F  (the “Refunded 
Bonds”) and to pay the costs of issuing the Series 20 20B Bonds and related costs.  The refunding will be 
accomplished by depositing in an irrevocable escrow fund (the “Escrow Fund”) with Regions Bank 
Corporate Trust, as escrow agent (the “Escrow Agent”),  cash and/or noncallable obligations of the United 
States government (“Government Obligations”) sufficient to pay all principal and interest when due on such 
Refunded Bonds through and until the date that they are irrevocably called for redemption by the City.  
Upon deposit of such amounts in the Escrow Fund,  such Refunded Bonds w ill no longer constitute 
outstanding obligations of the City, but will be paya ble solely from the Escrow Fund.  The accuracy of 
certain computations made in connection with the Refunded Bonds will be verified by the Verification 
Agent (herein defined).  See “VERIFICATION OF MATHEMATICAL COMPUTATIONS”  herein.   
 3 
See Appendix F for a summary of maturities, principal am ounts, redemption dates and redemption prices 
for the Refunded Bonds. 
The three pilot projects to be financed with the Series 2020A Bonds and the environmental 
objectives of the projects are described in general te rms below (the “Resiliency Projects”). Attached as 
Appendix E is a Technical  Memorandum (the “Project  Memorandum”) prepared by Quantified Ventures 
in association with the Chesapeake Bay Foundation on be half of the City which describes in greater detail 
the Resiliency Projects, their anticipated outcomes and the methodology behind certain evaluation metrics.  
The City has undertaken to make certain voluntary disclosure respecting the Resiliency Projects for a 
limited period of time. The City’s voluntary disclosure undertaking is summarized below under “LIMITED 
VOLUNTARY CONTINUING DISCLOSURE RELATED TO RESILENCY PROJECTS,” and described 
in the Project Technical Memorandum.  The City may modify, amend or discontinue the voluntary 
disclosure at any time in its sole discretion without the consent of the holders of the Series 2020A Bonds.. 
Designation of the Series 2020A Bonds as Environmental Impact Bonds and Green Bonds 
The City is designating the Series 2020A Bonds as “Environmental Impact Bonds” and “Green 
Bonds” based upon the environmental benefits of the sustainable mitigation and climate adaptation features 
of the Resiliency Projects to be financed with the proceeds of the Series 2020A Bonds, summarized herein.  
Further detail is included in Appendix E: Project Technical Memorandum.  The Resiliency Projects will 
become a part of the City stormwater manageme nt system upon completion a nd  will be operated and 
maintained by the City’s Public Works Department together with other components of the system.  
The City has identified flood mitigation as a goal of its ongoing resilienc y planning process. 
Hampton's low-lying geographic location, with many waterways, make much of the City extremely 
susceptible to flooding. There are two types of area s affected by flooding: areas within the designated 
floodplain, which are most likely to flood during inte nse rain events, and areas affected by storm surge 
flooding, which occurs when high winds and changes in air pressure push water toward the shore. Most of 
the City is in a surge zone. Both types of flooding create risks to public safety and property and can impede 
economic development and the quality of  life for residents of the City. Flood risks in the Hampton Roads 
region have also been linked to a combination of land subsidence and sea level rise. 
Resilient Hampton is a City initiative to bolster the City’s inherent strengths in order to alleviate 
chronic stresses and enable recovery from extreme even ts and shocks in ways that make the community 
even stronger than before, including but not limited to  severe weather, sea level rise, and recurrent tidal 
flooding, in such a way as to also improve residents’ quality of life, economic viability, and environmental 
health. Information on the initiative is available at https://hampton.gov/3459/Resilient-Hampton.   
Green Bonds.  The Green Bonds designation is designed to track the generally accepted Green 
Bond Principles promulgated by the International Capita l Market Association (“ICMA”). By reference to 
the ICMA’s “Green and Social Bonds: A High-Leve l Mapping to the Sustainable Development Goals” 
(June 2018), the City has determined that the desi gnation reflects the use of proceeds of the Series 2020A 
Bonds in a manner that is consis tent with “Goal 1: “No Poverty, ” “Goal 11: Sustainable Cities and 
Communities,” “Goal 13: Climate Action,” and “Goal 14:  Life Below Water” of the United Nations 17 
Sustainable Development Goals (referred to as “UNSDGs” generally and “SDG 1,”  “SDG 11,” “SDG 13,” 
and “SDG 14.” specifically). The Green Bond Principl es have four core components: Use of Proceeds, 
Process for Project Evaluation and Selection, Management of Proceeds, and Reporting. These components 
as applicable to the City and the Series 2020A Bonds are described further below.  Further alignment with 
the Green Bond Principles and UN SDGs is described in Appendix E: Project Technical Memorandum.  
 4 
Environmental Impact Bonds. The Environmental Impact Bonds designation reflects the fact that: 
(1) as with the Green Bonds designation, the proceeds of the Series 2020A Bonds will be used for projects 
creating environmental benefits; a nd (2) the environmental benefits of those funded projects will be 
objectively measured and publicly reported, including to the bondholders. The second feature, relating to 
the measurement of outcomes achieved, distinguishes Environmental Impact Bonds from traditional Green 
Bonds, for which environmental benefits may be est imated initially but may not necessarily be tracked, 
verified or reported to bondholders. The City, Quantified Ventures and the Chesapeake Bay Foundation 
have developed the Project Technical Memorandum , which details the methodology behind the pre-
construction estimation and post-c onstruction evaluation of the Prim ary Outcome Metric (as defined 
below). Appendix E includes the Project Technical Memorandum.  
The repayment obligations of the City with respect to the Series 2020A Bonds are not conditioned 
on the completion of any particular project or the satisfaction of any condition relating to the status of the 
Series 2020A Bonds as Environmental Impact Bonds or Green Bonds. The City does not assume any 
obligation to ensure compliance with any legal or other principles of Green Bonds as such principles may 
evolve over time. The terms Environmental Impact  Bonds and Green Bonds are used herein for 
identification purposes only and are not intended to prov ide or imply that holders of Series 2020A Bonds 
are entitled to any additional security or benefits other than as provided in the Resolution. 
Use of Series 2020A Bond Proceeds. The Series 2020A Bonds are being issued to finance the costs 
of the Resiliency Projects, which constitute green infrastructure and flood mitigation projects identified and 
undertaken pursuant to the Resilient Hampton initiative. The initial Resiliency Projects consist of three pilot 
projects—Big Bethel Blueway, North Armistead Ave nue, and Lake Hampton—described in further detail 
below. These Resiliency Projects are climate change  adaptation and flood mitigation projects to address 
both chronic and extreme flooding in the Newmarket Creek watershed. In addition, the projects are designed 
to provide a variety of community benefits.  
Big Bethel Blueway  is a project that will store and slow water through the redesign of existing 
waterways to reduce flooding upstream in residential neighborhoods, as well as downstream in Newmarket 
Creek. A series of weirs in the main channel will be installed to allow it to partially fill up and detain water 
during a storm event. The main drainage channel, as well as the many smaller backyard drainage runnels 
that feed into it, will be expanded to increase the st ormwater storage capacity. Installing vegetation on the 
banks will aid in the slowing and infiltration of wa ter, as well as creating visual privacy for adjacent 
residential parcels. The Blueway will have several  distinctive segments along its length, based on 
ecological, topographic, and urban typologies. As th e Blueway approaches Newmarket Creek, ecological 
typologies change to recreate ones found in coastal  Virginia, where each has a different planting and 
stormwater strategy. Separate funding is anticipated  to redesign the Blueway's existing maintenance path 
to become a recreational trail and to accommodate more stormwater storage.  
North Armistead Avenue is a major route for traffic through the City and serves as a major connector 
of economic drivers like Joint Base Langley-Eustis, downtown Hampton, and the Coliseum Central district. 
In addition, North Armistead Avenue connects to ke y evacuation routes. Because the project area is low-
lying, the roadway already sees frequent flood-relate d transportation disruptions, hampering reliable 
transportation to the military base and other key economic centers, and over time, this part of the roadway 
is projected to be increasingly vulnerable to inundati on. To mitigate this flood risk, a portion of North 
Armistead Avenue between  Findley Street and the bridge  just west of Lasalle Avenue will be raised by 
several feet. In connection with the road raising, green  infrastructure will be installed to help slow, store, 
and redirect stormwater within the space adjacent to th e elevated roadway. This system will be connected 
to the drainage improvements at Lake Hampton. Separate funding is planned to establish a shared use path 
along the raised road.   
 5 
Lake Hampton will be transformed from a retention pond into a stormwater park that increases the 
site’s existing stormwater storage capacity and yields multiple benefits for the community. The project will 
raise the height of the existing dam and weir and install a series of detention basins with wetland plantings 
in a “treatment train” to slow, store, and clean r unoff from North Armistead Avenue before it enters the 
lake. Separate funding is anticipated to extend the existing Water Walk trail as a multi-use trail around the 
lake, bringing people alongside Newmarket Creek. 
Outcomes.  The Resiliency Projects are expected to create significant new stormwater storage 
capacity that will mitigate flood risks to homes and to important transportation corridors like North 
Armistead Avenue. The Primary Outcome Metric for the Resiliency Projects will be Stormwater Volume 
Storage Capacity (gallons), the maximum volume of stormwater that the Resiliency Projects can store at a 
single point in time. Stormwater Volume Storag e Capacity represents a proxy measurement of the 
Resiliency Projects’ contribution to the City’s impr oved resilience profile. The City will engage a third-
party engineering firm to produce an evaluation and verification of the environmental performance of the 
Resiliency Projects at completion of each of the three projects, including the added stormwater storage 
capacity. That report will be disclosed to holders of the Series 2020A Bonds as described in Environmental 
Impact Bond and Green Bond Reporting, below.  
As a capacity measure, the Resiliency Projects’ storage capacity represents the maximum amount 
of stormwater they can store every time it rains. This  will especially benefit the City by absorbing chronic 
runoff during those less severe, more frequent rain events. In aggregate, the Resiliency Projects are expected 
to add 8.37 million gallons of Stormwater Volume St orage Capacity, or 25.7 acre-feet. One acre-foot is 
approximately equal to filling a football field with wate r to a height of one foot. The expected stormwater 
storage capacity of the Resiliency Projects as estimated by the City’s engineers using the project design is 
shown in the table below. 
Resiliency Project Expected Added Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity, 
gallons (acre-feet) 
Big Bethel Blueway  2.70 million gallons (8.3 acre-feet) 
North Armistead Avenue Road Raising 
and Green Infrastructure 
 0.33 million gallons (1.0 acre-feet) 
Lake Hampton  5.59 million gallons (17.1 acre-feet) 
Total  8.62 million gallons (26.4 acre-feet) 
The ability of the City to achieve the calculated outcomes through the Resiliency Projects is subject 
to certain risks and factors outside of the City’s control, including but not limited to unanticipated 
geotechnical site conditions, exogenous factors such  as weather events, social or labor conditions, 
availability of project materials, and similar matters.  
The North Armistead Avenue project is also expected to provide transportation resiliency benefits 
in addition to its climate adaptation benefits. Based on the current height of the road, water levels, and past 
traffic data, the road currently experiences an average of 92 hours of flooding per year, affecting 
approximately 45,000 vehicles annually (see Appendix E). Current projections indicate the project will 
eliminate annual recurring flooding and the associated transportation disruptions. 
The Resiliency Projects will benefit Black and low- and moderate-income communities.  According 
to data from the U.S. Census Bureau’s American Community Survey, Black residents comprise 51.2% of 
the population of the census tracts in the Newmarket Creek watershed; of those census tracts, 65% have 
median household incomes that are low-income (bel ow 80% of area median) or moderate-income (80- 
 6 
120% of area median). See Appendix E for selected socioeconomic data of the Newmarket Creek watershed 
census tracts. 
In addition, the Resiliency Projects will create added benefits, such as enhancing neighborhood 
access to recreational opportunities.  
Process for Project Evaluation and Selection . As part of the Resilient Hampton initiative, the 
City conducted a multi-year process to identify res iliency challenges and opportunities. Beginning in 
2015, the City engaged in the “Dutch Dialogues Virginia,” a workshop that brought together urban water 
management experts from New Orleans and the Nether lands —engineers, architects, and planners — to 
meet with their counterparts in Hampton Roads to di scuss ideas and solutions for the future. After that 
meeting, the City pledged to be a leader in coastal resiliency. Since then, Hampton has engaged Waggoner 
& Ball Architects (a firm based in New Orleans w ho were lead participants in the Dutch Dialogues 
workshop) to help gather data and develop an integrated approach to coastal resiliency, based on the Dutch 
approach to life at sea level and the motto “Living Wi th Water.” Rather than look at flooding alone, this 
approach helps integrate flood risk mitigation, engineering, urban design, environmental restoration, 
community assets, and economic development. 
The City Council selected the Newmarket Creek watershed as the first pilot project area. In January 
2019, Waggonner & Ball and their Dutch colleagues from BoschSlabbers convened in Hampton for a week-
long design workshop that included stakeholders from a range of organizations including Old Dominion 
University, Hampton University, the U.S. Army Co rps of Engineers, Newport News and Poquoson 
officials, the Hampton Roads Planning District  Commission, Joint Base Langley–Eustis, NASA, the 
Virginia Institute of Marine Science (VIMS), th e Virginia Marine Resources Commission (VMRC) and 
more. The purpose of this workshop was to develop pr oject proposals and areas of the City where water 
can be managed before it reaches Newmar ket Creek. One of the key principles  was to find ways to slow, 
store, and redirect water to reduce flooding impacts in ways consistent with the Resilient Hampton 
principles of promoting sustainability and the prot ection and incorporation of natural resources into 
flooding responses. Projects were envisioned all along the creek and throughout the watershed.  
The conceptual designs prioritized during the wo rkshop included the Resiliency Projects selected 
for funding through the Series 2020A Bonds by the City’s Finance, Planning, and Public Works 
departments with the approval of the City Council. The Resiliency Projects were selected for funding based 
on their contribution towards improving the City’s resiliency profile under the Resilient Hampton initiative, 
their capacity for storing stormwater, which is a ma jor factor in both chronic and extreme flooding, and 
their ability to improve living conditions and reduce flooding in low-income neighborhoods in the affected 
watershed.  
Management of Series 2020A Bond Proceeds . The net proceeds of the Series 2020A Bonds 
remaining after payment of issuance costs will be used by the City to pay costs of the Resiliency Projects. 
Such amounts will be tracked and disbursed for such purpose and, pending disbursement, will be invested 
in accordance with Virginia law. 
Environmental Impact Bond and Green Bond Reporting. The City expects to report annually on 
a voluntary basis on the expenditures of the proceed s of the Series 2020A Bonds and the construction 
progress of the Resiliency Projects through completion of all Resiliency Projects. This report will also 
identify the Resiliency Projects’ alignment with the U. N. Sustainable Development Goals. The City will 
also engage a third-party engineering firm to produ ce an evaluation and verification of the environmental 
performance of the Resiliency Projects, at project  completion. Following completion of each of the 
Resiliency Projects, the City plans to provide a one -time, voluntary disclosure detailing the measured and  
 7 
independently verified outcome metric described  above in the table captioned “Expected Added 
Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity.” The City’s voluntary disclosure undertaking is further described 
under “LIMITED VOLUNTARY CONTINUING DISCLOSURE RELATED TO RESILENCY 
PROJECTS” and in the Project Technical Memorandum. The City may modify, amend or discontinue the 
voluntary disclosure at any time in its sole discretion without the consent of the holders of the Series 2020A 
Bonds. 
Additional Funding. The City expects to apply for federal a nd other grants to provide additional  
funding for the Resiliency Projects and other stormwat er management projects.  Any additional funding 
will not impact the development and construction or goals and objectiv es of the Resiliency Projects, the 
designation of the Series 2020A Bonds as Environmental Impact Bonds and Green Bonds or the City’s 
expectation to provide limited vol untary continuing disclosure on the Resiliency Projects as described 
above.  
General 
The full faith and credit of the City are irrevocab ly pledged to the payment of the Bonds.  The 
Bonds will be dated as of their date of issue.  The Bonds will bear interest from their dated date at the rates 
per annum set forth on the inside c over page hereof.  Interest will be  calculated on the basis of a 360-day 
year of twelve 30-day months, payable on March 1 and September 1, commencing March 1, 2021, until the 
earlier of maturity or redemption (each an “Interest Payment Date”).  The Bonds will mature on the dates 
and in the principal amounts set forth on the inside cover page hereof. 
Book-Entry Only System 
The Bonds will be issued in fully registered fo rm in the denominations of $5,000 and multiples 
thereof and will be held by DTC or its nominee, as securities depository with respect to the Bonds.  See 
Appendix D  “Book-Entry Only System” to this Official Statement for a description of the book-entry 
system.  Purchases of beneficial ownership interests in the Bonds will be made only in book-entry form and 
individual purchasers will not receive physical delivery of bond certificates.  As long as the Bonds are held 
by DTC or its nominee, principal, premium, if any, and interest will be paid to Cede & Co., as nominee of 
DTC, in same day funds on each Interest Payment Date by Regions Bank, as registrar and paying agent (the 
“Paying Agent”).  If the book-entry system is discontinued, bond certificates will be delivered as described 
in the Resolution, and Beneficial Owners (as defined in Appendix D) will become registered owners of the 
Bonds (“Bondholders”).  Principal, premium, if any,  and interest on the Bonds shall be payable on each 
Interest Payment Date by check or draft mailed to the registered owners at each owner’s address as it 
appears on the fifteenth day of the month preceding an Interest Payment Date on the registration books kept 
by the Paying Agent.

REDEMPTION PROVISIONS 
Redemption 
Optional Redemption.  The Series 2020A Bonds maturing on or after September 1, 2031 may be 
redeemed prior to their respective maturities, at the option of the City, from any moneys that may be made 
available for such purpose, either in whole or in part (in $5,000 increments), on and after September 1, 2030 
at any time and in such order as the City may determine at a redemption price equal to 100% of the principal 
amount to be redeemed together with the interest accrued thereon to the date fixed for redemption. 
No Redemption of the Series 2020B Bonds.  The Series 2020B Bonds may not be redeemed prior 
to maturity. 
Manner of Redemption 
If less than all of the Series 2020A Bonds are ca lled for redemption, such Series 2020A Bonds to 
be redeemed shall be selected by the City’s Director of Finance in such a manner as he or she may determine 
to be in the best interest of the City.  If less than all of the Series 2020A Bonds of a particular maturity are 
called for redemption, such Series 2020A Bonds to be redeemed shall be selected by DTC or any successor 
securities depository pursuant to its rules and proce dures or, if the book-entry system is discontinued, by 
the Paying Agent by lot in such manner as the Paying Agent in its discretion may determine.  In either case, 
(a) the portion of any Series 2020A Bond to be redeemed shall be in the principal amount of $5,000 or some 
multiple thereof and (b  in selecting Series 2020A Bonds for redemption, each Series 2020A Bond shall be 
considered as representing that number of Series 2020A Bonds which is obtained by dividing the principal 
amount of such Series 2020A Bond by $5,000. 
Notice of Redemption 
The Paying Agent, upon being satisfied as to the pa yment of its expenses, shall send notice of the 
call for redemption, identifying the Series 2020A Bonds or portions thereof to be redeemed, not less than  
 9 
30 nor more than 60 days prior to the redemption da te (a) by facsimile, registered or certified mail or 
overnight express delivery, to the holder of each such  Series 2020A Bond to be redeemed at the holder’s 
address as it  appears on the registration books kept by the Paying Agent, and (b) to the MSRB via its 
EMMA system (as such terms are defined herein) or such other MSRB system then in effect for such 
purpose. In the City’s discretion, any such notice of redemption may condition redemption of Series 2020A 
Bonds on the  receipt or availability of funds for such purpose.  
The City shall not be responsible for mailing noti ce of redemption to anyone other than DTC or 
another qualified securities depository or its nomin ee unless no qualified securities depository is the 
registered owner of the Series 2020A Bonds.  If no qu alified securities depository is the registered owner 
of the Series 2020A Bonds, notice of redemption shall be mailed to the registered owners of the Series 
2020A  Bonds.  During the period that DTC or the DTC nominee is the registered holder of the Series 
2020A Bonds, the City will not be responsible for mailing notices of redemption to the beneficial 
owners of the Series 2020A Bonds.  See “Book-Entry Only System” in Appendix D. 
SECURITY FOR THE BONDS 
The Bonds are general obligations of the City, to  the payment of which the City’s full faith and 
credit are irrevocably pledged.  The City Council is  authorized and required by law, unless funds are 
lawfully available and appropriated for timely payment of the Bonds, to levy and collect, at the same time 
and in the same manner as other taxes of the City are assessed, levied and collected, an ad valorem tax, over 
and above all other taxes authorized or limited by law and unlimited as to rate or amount, upon all property 
subject to taxation by the City sufficient to pay the pr incipal of and premium, if any, and interest on the 
Bonds, which tax shall be in addition to all other taxes authorized to be levied in the City. 
BONDHOLDERS’ REMEDIES IN THE EVENT OF DEFAULT 
Section 15.2-2659 of the Code of Virginia of 1 950, as amended, provides that upon an affidavit 
filed with the Governor of the Commonwealth by petiti on of any owner of or paying agent for a general 
obligation bond or note in default as to payment of prin cipal, premium, if any, or interest, the Governor 
shall conduct a summary investigation and, if satisfied that such default has occurred, the Governor shall 
order the State Comptroller to withhold all funds appropriated and payable by the Commonwealth to the 
locality issuing such general obligation bond or note and apply the amount so wit hheld to payment of the 
defaulted principal, premium and interest. 
Section 15.2-2659 also provides for notice to the re gistered owners of such bonds or notes of the 
default and the availability of with held funds.  The State Comptroller advises that, to date, no order to 
withhold funds pursuant to Section 15.2-2659, or its predecessor provisions, Sections 15.1-225 and 15.1-
227.61, has ever been issued.  Although neither Section 15.2-2659 nor its predecessor provisions, Sections 
15.1-225 and 15.1-227.61, has been approved by a Virgin ia court, the Attorney General of Virginia has 
determined that appropriated funds can  be withheld pursuant to its predecessor section.  In the fiscal year 
ended June 30, 2020 total direct appropriations paid by the Commonwealth to the City, excluding payments 
for public school purposes, amounted to approximately  $69,695,232 (unaudited) of which approximately 
$57,191,237 (unaudited) was deposited in the City's Ge neral Fund, approximately $3,054,993(unaudited) 
was deposited to the City’s Capital Projects Fund and the balance, approximately $9,449,002 (unaudited), 
to the Federal Grants Fund.  The City has never defaulted on the payment of principal, premium or interest 
on any of its general obligation bonds or notes. 
Neither the Bonds nor the proceedings with respect  thereto specifically provide any remedies that 
would be available to a noteholder or bondholder if th e City defaults in the payment of principal of,  
 10 
premium or interest on the Bonds, nor do they contain any provision for the appointment of a trustee to 
protect and enforce the interests of the bondholders or  noteholders upon the occurrence of such a default.  
Upon any default in the payment of principal of, pr emium or interest, a bondholder or noteholder may, 
among other things, seek to obtain from an appropriate court a writ of mandamus requiring the City Council 
to levy and collect taxes as described above.  The mandamus remedy, however, may be impracticable and 
difficult to enforce.  Furthermore, the right to enforce payment of the Bonds may be limited by bankruptcy, 
insolvency, reorganization, moratorium and similar laws and equitable principles, which may limit the 
specific enforcement of certain remedies. 
Chapter 9 of the United States Bankruptcy Code (t he “Bankruptcy Code”) permits a municipality 
such as the City, if insolvent or otherwise unable to pay its debts as they become due, to file a voluntary 
petition for the adjustment of debts provided that su ch municipality is “specifically authorized, in its 
capacity as a municipality or by name, to be a debtor ...” Bankruptcy Code, §109(c)(2).  Current Virginia 
statutes do not expressly authorize the City or municipalities generally to file for bankruptcy under Chapter 
9.  Chapter 9 does not authorize the filing of involuntary petitions against municipalities such as the City. 
If Virginia law were changed or interpreted to au thorize a municipality to file for bankruptcy, any 
bankruptcy proceedings by the City could have adverse effects on holders of the Bonds including (a) delay 
in the enforcement of their remedies, (b) subordination of their claims to claims of those supplying goods 
and services to the City after the initiation of bankruptcy proceedings and to the administrative expenses of 
bankruptcy proceedings, and (c) impos ition without their consent of a reorganization plan reducing or 
delaying payment of the Bonds.  The Bankruptcy Code c ontains provisions intended to ensure that, in any 
reorganization plan not accepted by at least a majority of a class of creditors such as the holders of general 
obligation bonds and notes, such cred itors will have the benefit of their original claim or the “indubitable 
equivalent.” The effect of these and other provisions of the Bankruptcy Code cannot be predicted and may 
be significantly affected by judicial interpretations and subsequent legislation. 
PART II 
 
CITY INDEBTEDNESS 
AUTHORIZATION OF DEBT 
There is no requirement in the Virginia Constitution,  the Virginia statutes or in the Charter of the 
City that the issuance of general obligation bonds of the City be subject to approval of the electors of the 
City at referendum.  The City Council has full author ity to authorize and issue general obligation bonds 
such as the Bonds. 
The only constitutional limitation on the issuance of  general obligation bon ds is contained in 
Article VII, Section 10 of the Virginia Constitution, which states that: 
“No city or town shall issue any bonds or other interest-bearing obligations 
which, including existing indebtedness,  shall at any time exceed ten per 
centum of the assessed valuation of real estate in the city or town subject 
to taxation, as shown by the last preceding assessment for taxes.” 
The legal debt margin as of June 30, 2020 is com puted in the following table, and upon issuance 
of the Bonds, the City will remain in compliance with the Virginia law.

OVERLAPPING JURISDICTION DEBT 
The City is a separate and distinct governmental unit, autonomous from any county, town or other 
political subdivision of the Commonwealth of Virginia.  The City is not subject to taxation by any county, 
school district or other political subdivision, and is liable only for indebtedness which the City incurs.

Of the general obligation bonds, the City issued $5,670,000 in aggregate principal amount as  Build 
America Bonds (“BABs”) and $1,520,000 in aggreg ate principal amount as Recovery Zone Economic 
Development Bonds (“RZEDBs”), all of which are outs tanding.  The general obligation bonds issued on 
behalf of the School Board of the City of Hampton,  VA (the “School Board”), of which $1,050,000 is 
outstanding, were issued as Qualified School Constr uction Bonds (“QSCBs”).  When issued, interest 
payments on the BABs were subject to a 35% federa l subsidy payment, and interest payments on the  
 13 
RZEDBs were subject to a 45% fede ral subsidy payment.  Due to fe deral budget sequestration, federal 
subsidy payments have been subject to reduction since March 2013. The QSCBs holders receive a federal 
tax credit in lieu of interest payments; thus the City  pays no interest on such bonds.  The federal subsidy 
payments are a small portion of the City’s debt service payment and, if not paid, will have no impact on the 
City’s ability to make payments when due. 
CITY MORAL OBLIGATIONS 
The City has provided its moral obligation to various financing arrangements shown in Table 3 for 
public purposes.  A moral obligation is a non-binding obligation of the City to consider making up deficits 
on certain obligations from monies available in the City’s General Fund.  In the event the revenues pledged 
or dedicated to the payment of the revenue bonds listed in Table 3 below are insufficient to pay debt service, 
the City has agreed to include in its annual budge t, subject to appropriation by City Council, amounts 
sufficient to cure any such deficit.  Such bonds are not general obligations of the City and are not secured 
by the City’s full faith and credit and taxing power.  The average aggregate annual debt service payable by 
the City on its moral obligation commitments listed in Table 3 through their scheduled maturities is 
approximately $6,519,673.

On November 20, 2019, the City issued its $66,150,000 Convention Center Refunding Revenue 
Bond, Series 2019 (Taxable) to the Virginia Resources Authority (“VRA”) with interest rates from 1.952% 
to 3.097% (the “2019 VRA Bond”). The proceeds of the 2019 VRA Bond were used to refund all of the 
City’s Convention Center Refundi ng Revenue Bond, Series 2012 and all but $6.3 million of the City’s 
Convention Center Refunding Revenue Bond, Seri es 2011 (the “2011 VRA Bond), both of which were 
held by VRA.  $3,270,000 in principal is currently  outstanding on the 2011 VRA Bond which is due and 
payable on October 1, 2021.  Interest on the 2019 VRA Bond is due and payable semi-annually on each 
April 1 and October 1. Principal on the 2019 VRA Bond is payable annually on October 1  through maturity 
in October 2034. The 2019 VRA Bond and 2011 VRA Bond (collectively, the “VRA Bonds”) are secured 
by a pledge of net revenue genera ted from the operation of the Convention Center and from investment 
earnings on reserve funds established for such bonds.  Debt service on the VRA Bonds is paid in part and 
primarily from a portion of the meal and hotel taxes im posed by the City (2% of the City’s 7.5% meal tax 
and 2% of the City’s 8% hotel tax).  Such taxes were earmarked by the City for the payment of debt service 
on bonds issued in 2002 to fund the development and construction of the Convention Center project.  While 
such tax revenue is not formally pl edged to pay debt service, the 2% in crease in the hotel and meal taxes 
has provided a revenue stream to pay the majority of the debt service on the VRA Bonds and bonds refunded 
by such bonds. Additional support for the payment of debt service on the VRA Bonds is provided by the 
City’s agreement to cure any debt service deficit on the VRA Bonds, subject to the appropriation of funds  
 14 
for such purpose by City Council.  In fiscal year 2020 (unaudited), the City paid $6.6 million towards debt 
service from revenues generated by the meal tax and hotel tax increase.  The City also contributed 
approximately $636,000 to the Convention Center to cover operating deficits in fiscal year 2020. 
The City entered into a limited moral obligation pl edge in connection with a 20-year tax-exempt 
lease revenue bonds issued on September 7, 2001 by th e Economic Development Authority of the City of 
Hampton, VA (the “Economic Development Authority” or “EDA”) in the amount of $9,270,419 to pay off 
the construction note associated with the Peninsula Workforce Development Center.  The debt is secured 
by 20-year leases with Old Dominion University , Thomas Nelson Community College (TNCC), the 
Virginia Employment Commission (VEC) and the Greater Peninsula Workforce Development Consortium 
(Consortium).  Under the City’s limited moral obliga tion, the City may be requested to make up any 
deficiencies of revenues necessary to cover the cost of  repair or replacement, if insurance proceeds are 
insufficient, in the case of damage, condemnation or loss of title of all or any part of the project; repair and 
restoration costs that are the responsibility of the EDA but undertaken by a lessee in the event of casualty, 
condemnation or loss of title; and rent under the VEC or Consortium leases upon termination until replaced 
by a new lease satisfactory to the lender (VEC and Consortium have a 30-day notice of termination right).  
To date, rental payments have been sufficient to pay debt service on the EDA’s bonds, and the City has not 
been requested to fund any debt ser vice payments or operating expenses.  In addition, the City and five 
other local governments entered into a funding agreement with the State Board for Community Colleges to 
provide an annual local match, subject to appropria tion, for 20 years to correspond with TNCC’s lease in 
the facility.  The City has paid $73,500 annually for the past 16 years. 
OTHER LONG-TERM COMMITMENTS 
In February 2006, the City Council establis hed the Peninsula Town Center Community 
Development Authority (the “PTC CDA”).  The PTC CDA was established to help finance infrastructure 
improvements to the Peninsula Town Center Mall site which was being re developed, and which 
redevelopment continues,  into a mixed-use facility w ith retail and residential areas.  The City agreed to 
levy and collect a special assessment and special tax on the property within the PTC CDA District.  
Additionally, the City agreed to pledge, subject to  annual appropriation by the City Council, certain 
collected incremental tax revenues (real estate tax (100%), sales tax (50%),  food and beverage tax (25%), 
and amusement admissions tax (50%)) from within the PTC CDA District  in support of the bonds issued 
by the PTC CDA.  The incremental tax revenues are dete rmined from a fixed base of collected revenue in 
fiscal year 2006 for real estate taxes and in  calendar year 2004 for sales, food and beverage, and amusement 
admissions taxes.  In September 2007, the PTC C DA issued $92,850,000 of revenue bonds (the “CDA 
Bonds”) which are payable from the special assessment, the special tax and incremental tax revenues to the 
extent collected and appropriated by the City Council.  In August 2018, the PTC CDA issued $77,880,000 
of refunding bonds to refund the Series 2007 bonds a nd extend the maturity of the 2018 Bonds to 2045, 
eight years beyond the 2037 maturity of the Seri es 2007 Bonds.  The CDA Bonds, including any bonds 
issued by the PTC CDA to refund the CDA Bonds, are not  a debt of the City nor are they considered a 
contingent liability of the City.  
SHORT-TERM OBLIGATIONS 
The City may issue various types of short-term debt obligations, including bond anticipation notes 
and tax and revenue anticipation notes. The City has no bond or revenue anticipation notes outstanding. 
The City has entered into short-term lease/purchase financing arrangements to finance vehicles and 
other equipment for City use of which approximate ly $2.9 million was outstanding as of June 30, 2020 
(unaudited). The equipment lease/purchase financings ar e secured by the equipment financed.  The City’s  
 15 
obligation to make payments under the financing leases is subject to the annual appropriation of funds for 
such purpose by City Council.  The equipment lease/ purchase financings are not debt for purposes of the 
Virginia Constitution, and therefore are not general ob ligations of the City secured by its full faith and 
credit.

FINANCIAL POLICY GUIDELINES 
In April 2007, the City Council amended its self-i mposed guidelines originally adopted in April 
1986 for future financial policy making, officially named Financial Policy Guidelines.  The purpose of the 
Financial Policy Guidelines is to serve as an additio nal check and balance on the City’s existing financial 
structure.  The guidelines address aspects of debt management, a minimum percentage relationship between 
unassigned fund balance and revenues in the General Fund at the end of each fiscal year, and minimum 
requirements for equity funding of capital projects.  The original guidelines became effective in the fiscal 
year beginning July 1, 1987.  The amended guidelines became effective upon adoption in April 2007. 
As a component of the Financial Policy Guidelines, the City has adopted debt policies which place 
further limitations on its ability to incur debt in addition to the debt limit established by Virginia law.  The 
Financial Policy Guidelines, including the debt policies are as follows: 
1. Debt Policy Limit.  (a) General obligation debt shall not exceed 3% of the assessed value 
of all real estate within the City subject to taxation; 
(b) General obligation debt together with indir ect debt, which includes certain revenue backed 
debt and subject-to-appropriation or moral obligation commitments, a nd debt of certain special purpose 
entities shall not exceed 4.5% of th e assessed value of all real and pe rsonal property subject to taxation 
within the City; and 
(c) Debt of certain special purpose entities, su ch as community development authorities, shall 
not exceed 1% of the assessed value of all real and personal property subject to taxation within the City. 
2. Debt Service Policy Limit.   General obligation debt and indirect debt shall not exceed 
10% of the City’s total general fund expenditures plus expenditures of the City’s public school system.  
 16 
3. General Obligation Debt Retirement Policy.  The City shall retire 60% of general 
obligation debt within 10 years of the date the debt is incurred. 
4. Equity Funding.   A minimum of 2% to 6% of gene ral fund revenues will be applied to 
capital projects in the CIP (defined below) in each year and a minimum of 10% to 15% of CIP projects over 
a rolling five-year period are to be funded from general fund revenues. 
5. Unassigned Fund Balance.   The City will maintain an unassigned general fund balance 
equal to 10% of total general fund and school operating fund revenues, and any expenditures which reduce 
the balance below the 10% policy requirement shall be replenished over a three-year period. 
The City is in compliance with the Financial Policy Guidelines. 
LONG-TERM BORROWING PROGRAM 
The City prepares a Capital Improvements Plan  (the “CIP”) which provides a method for 
implementing the long-range development, rehabilitation and renovation of City facilities and capital assets.  
The CIP is updated annually for a five-year period.  Funding sources include long-term debt issuance, City 
current revenues, a Wastewater and Stormwater Fund supported from certain taxes and fees, as well as land 
sales, to fund specific projects financed by such Funds, and revenues from the federal and state 
governments. 
PART III 
 
FINANCIAL INFORMATION 
BASIS OF ACCOUNTING AND ACCOUNTING PROCEDURES 
Accounting and Reporting Practices 
The accounts of the City are organized on the basis of funds, each of which is considered to be a 
separate accounting entity.  The opera tions of each fund are accounted fo r by providing a separate set of 
self-balancing accounts which comprise each fund’s asset s, deferred outflows of resources, liabilities, 
deferred inflows of resources, fund balance or net position, revenues and expend itures or expenses.  The 
following is a description of the funds included in th e financial statements acc ompanying this Official 
Statement. 
General Fund - accounts for all revenues and expenditures of the City which are not accounted for 
in the other funds.  Revenues are primarily derived from general property taxes, other local taxes, revenues 
from other governmental units and recovered costs. 
A significant part of the General Fund’s revenue is transferred to other funds, principally to fund 
the operation of the City public schools and the debt service requirements for the City. 
Expenditures include, among other things, those fo r general government, public safety, highways 
and streets, culture and recreation; health, and human services.  
 17 
Debt Service Fund 
The Debt Service Fund accounts for the accumulati on of resources that are restricted, committed 
or assigned to expenditures for principal and interest .  Primary resources of the Debt Service Fund are 
derived from transfers from the General Fund and the proceeds from the issuance of refunding bonds. 
Capital Projects Fund 
The Capital Projects Fund accounts for all general City and School construction projects and reports 
financial resources that are restricted, committed or assigned to expenditures for capital outlays which are 
financed through general obligation debt and general operating funds, with the exception of projects related 
to Enterprise and Internal Service Funds. 
Special Revenue Funds 
Special Revenue Funds account for and report the proceeds of specific revenue sources that are 
restricted or committed to expenditure for specific purposes other than debt service or capital projects.  The 
Federal Grants Fund accounts for revenues and expenditures of all federal, state and recovery act grants to 
the City except the Community Development entitleme nt.  The Federal Grants Fund also accounts for 
program income and matching requirements related to all awarded City grants.  Federal grants received by 
the Component Unit - School Board are included in  the discretely presented component unit. The 
Community Development Fund accounts for revenues and expenditures related to entitlements received by 
the City under the Community Development Act of 1974, administrated by the U.S. Department of Housing 
and Urban Development, to foster the development of the Healthy Neighborhoods Initiative.  Other 
revenues include program income that is generated from repayments of loans, the sale of rehabilitated 
homes and the Revolving Loan Fund. The Economic De velopment Fund was established to finance the 
costs of capital investments, improvements, expenditures and any other approved expenditures which will 
assist economic development and improve the physical  appearance of the City. Revenues are earned from 
land sales, special assessments, projects financed by the fund and rental receipts.  The Stormwater 
Management Fund accounts for revenues and expenditures related to maintaining the storm drainage system 
and quality of our surface waters. Revenues are deri ved from a service charge levied on all developed 
property in the City.  The Pembroke Complex Fund acc ounts for the receipts and disbursements related to 
the operations of the Pembroke Community Servi ce Center. The facility is owned by the Hampton 
Redevelopment and Housing Authorit y and maintained by the City from the rents charged to the various 
tenants of the building.  The Law Library Fund accounts for receipts and disbursements of funds specifically 
held for the purpose of purchasing law books.  Revenues are derived from a court case fee, which is legally 
mandated to support the operations of the law library. 
Enterprise Funds 
Enterprise Funds are used to account for operations (a) that are financed and operated in a manner 
similar to private business enterprises where the intent of the governing body is that costs of operating and 
providing services to the general public are completely or  partially financed from user fees; or (b) where 
the governing body has determined that the periodic determination of net results of operations is appropriate 
for management control and accountability. 
The City’s Enterprise Funds account for the operations of the Hampton Coliseum, The Woodlands 
Golf Course, The Hamptons (Golf Course), Solid W aste, the Virginia Air and Space Center (Museum), 
Convention Center, Wastewater Management, and Refuse-Steam Plant.  Revenues of the Hampton  
 18 
Coliseum, Woodlands Golf Course, The Hamptons (Golf Course), the Solid Waste Fund and the 
Wastewater Management Fund are derived primarily from user fees. 
The Museum’s operating revenue is derived from donations and user fees, including federal funds 
from the National Aeronautics and Space Administrati on for educational/user fee subsidy.  The fund 
accounts for all operational activities of the Museum. 
The Convention Center Fund revenues are derived from user fees and meal and lodging taxes. 
The Refuse-Steam Plant Fund accounts for the operati on of the City’s steam generating plant.  
Revenues are derived from the sale of steam to the Un ited States Government, a solid waste disposal fee 
(tipping fee) charged to the Solid Waste Fund and user fees charged to external customers. 
Internal Service Funds 
The Internal Service Funds account for the services provided to substantially all City departments 
or other governmental units on a cost-reimbursement  basis.  The City’s internal service funds include 
Equipment Replacement, Fleet Services, Risk Management, and Information Technology. 
The Equipment Replacement Fund owns all on-ro ad rolling stock of the City and leases the 
equipment to the various operating departments.  Leasing income is used for replacement of the equipment. 
The Fleet Services Fund accounts for the operation of  the City’s central automotive maintenance 
service facility. 
The Risk Management Fund accounts for the costs of providing insurance coverage and certain 
self-insured liabilities of the City and the School Board. 
The Information Technology Fund accounts for th e costs of providing the following services: 
personal computer support, and telecommunications services. 
Fiduciary Funds 
Fiduciary Funds are used to account for assets held by the City in a trustee capacity or as an agent 
for individuals, private organizations  and other governmental units.  Th e Fiduciary Funds consist of the 
Hampton Employees’ Retirement System (HERS), Special Welfare and Other Agency Funds. 
HERS pension trust fund accounts for the revenues and expenditures related to the City-sponsored 
retirement plan, which is administered by a board of  trustees appointed by City Council.  The Special 
Welfare Fund is an agency fund that accounts for receipts and disbursements for special assistance programs 
and activities sponsored by the City’s Social Servi ces department.  The Other Agency Fund accounts for 
amounts held in trust by the City for others such as the Hampton Roads Peninsula Drug Initiative. 
Discretely Presented Component Units 
Discretely presented component units are entities that  are legally separate from the City, but for 
which the City is financially accountable, and whose relationship with the City is such that exclusion would 
cause the City’s financial statements to be mislead ing or incomplete. The discretely presented component 
units are: the School Board of the City, the Economic Development Authority, Coliseum Central Business 
Improvement District, Inc., and Downtown Hampton Development Partnership, Inc.    
 19 
The School Board is a legally separate entity which oversees the operations of and establishes 
educational policies for the City’s public school sy stem.  Seven School Board members are elected by 
voters of the City of Hampton.  The School Board has no power to levy taxes or issue bonds.  Its budget is 
subject to approval by the City Council; however, C ouncil only approves, rejects, or modifies the single 
amount requested by the School Board.  The City provides operating and capital funding to the School 
Board.  The School Board has complete discretionary  authority to expend the amount appropriated by the 
City. 
The Hampton Economic Development Authority (EDA) is a political subdivision of the 
Commonwealth established under Industrial Developm ent and Revenue Bond Act of the Commonwealth 
of Virginia, together with ordinances adopted by th e City Council.  The EDA was established to promote 
and develop trade within the City  by inducing manufacturing, industrial and commercial enterprises to 
locate or remain in the City.  The City Council funds EDA’s operating budget and receives all revenues 
derived from EDA land sales. 
The Coliseum Central Business Improvement District, Inc. was incorporated on January 22, 1996, 
to promote, develop and market the Coliseum Central Business District in the City.  The Coliseum Central 
Business Improvement District was created by acti on of the City Council under provisions of the Code of 
Virginia.  An additional tax is assessed on commercial real estate located in the Coliseum District to provide 
funds for the economic development of the District.   
The Downtown Hampton Development Partnership, Inc. was incorporated on August 15, 1995, to 
promote, develop, and market the Downtown Hamp ton Business Improvement District. The Downtown 
Hampton Business Improvement District was created by action of the City Council under provisions of the 
Code of Virginia.  The City assesses an additional tax on the value of the commercial real estate located in 
the Downtown Hampton District to provide funds for the economic development of the District.   
INDEPENDENT AUDIT 
The City Charter requires an annual audit of th e City by independent certified public accountants 
selected by the City Council.  Cherry Bekaert L.L.P.  served as the City’s independent certified public 
accountants for the fiscal year ended June 30, 2019, a nd the Basic Financial Statements for such year are 
included in Appendix A to this Official Statement.  Cherry Bekaert L.L.P. will not be reviewing any matters 
in connection with the issuance and sale of the Bonds. 
The financial and operating data contained in Appendix A are as of the dates and for the periods 
indicated, which were prior to the outbreak of the COVID-19 pandemic.  Such financial and operating data 
have not been updated to reflect any potential imp acts of the COVID-19 pandemic on the City’s general 
economic and financial condition.  See “GENER AL FUND EXPENDITURES – Financial Operations: 
Preliminary (Unaudited) Fiscal Year 2020 Performance – General Fund Budgetary Comparison Fiscal Year 
2020 and Fiscal Year 2021” and “IMPACT OF COVID-19 ON OPERATIONS AND FINANCIAL 
CONDITION” for  financial information related to the impact of the pandemic on the City. 
BUDGETING PROCEDURES 
Prior to the beginning of each fiscal year, the City Council adopts a ba lanced operating budget 
consisting of contemplated expenditures and estimated revenues for the ensuing fiscal year.  This adopted 
budget is the basis of appropriate expenditures for the various functions and services of the City and for 
estimated revenues.  
 20 
The City’s budget development process commences in the latter part of the preceding calendar year 
when department leaders submit their preliminary funding requests to the Budget Division.  These requests, 
as well as other expenditure needs, i.e. capital im provements, are reviewed along with all sources of 
revenues, including proposed tax rates sufficient to meet the revenue needs contemplated in the budget. 
The review takes place during a series of work sessions convened by the Budget Review Committee 
(“BRC”).  The BRC consists of the City Manager, Assistant City Managers, City Attorney, Budget 
Manager, Finance Director, Human Resources Direct or, a representative from the Hampton City Schools 
(“HCS”) and rotating representatives from the City’s various Business Teams.  A recommended budget is 
submitted to City Council on or before April 15 for the fiscal year commencing the following July 1. 
Work sessions and public hearings are held by City Council prior to enactment of the final budget.  
Prior to June 1, the final budget is legally enacted through the passage of the annual appropriation ordinance. 
During the fiscal year, the budget is constantly monitored by department leaders, the Budget 
Division and the Finance Department.  Hampton’s City  Code authorizes the City Manager or the Budget 
Director to transfer any unencum bered appropriation balance from one (1) department, office, agency, 
project, program, or purpose to another department, office, agency, project, program, or purpose within the 
same accounting fund.  These budget transfers, as well as those that alter the total expenditures for a 
department, are submitted to City Council for notification and approval, respectively.  Transfers within the 
School Operating Fund are under the control of the School Board. 
The City is required by Virginia law to maintain  a balanced budget in each fiscal year based on 
anticipated revenues of the current year. 
The City Council adopted the budget for fiscal year 2021 on May 13, 2020.  See “GENERAL 
FUND – General Fund Budgetary Comparison” below fo r a  comparison in general terms of the major 
differences between the budgets for fiscal years 2020 and 2021.  
GENERAL GOVERNMENTAL REVENUES

revenues are paid into the General Fund.  The C ity’s General Fund revenues are derived from property 
taxes, other ad valorem taxes, other local taxes, licenses, fees , permits, certain revenues from the Federal 
and State governments, interest earned on invested cash balances and other revenues.  The principal sources 
of City revenue available to meet operating expenditures are presented below.

general property taxes provided $166.3 million out of total tax revenues of $248.1 million.  Other local 
taxes, including sales and use taxes,  provided $81.8 million.  The following table shows tax revenues by

Miscellaneous Revenues 
In addition to tax revenues, the City receives amounts in the City’s General Fund from a number 
of miscellaneous sources, including license permits and privilege fees, fines and forfeitures, revenues from 
use of money and property (including interest on i nvestments), and charges from services and recovered 
costs from the National Aeronautics and Space Admini stration (“NASA”).  In fiscal year 2019, revenue 
from these sources totaled $24 million, as compared with $25 million in fiscal year 2018. 
State Aid 
The City receives money from the State for non-cat egorical aid, shared expense for certain 
constitutional offices and categorical aid (for certain mandated programs, including public assistance and 
street and highway maintenance). Categorical State Aid also includes pass-thru federal funding (federal 
funding to localities which passes through the State) .  In fiscal years 2019 and 2018 these payments 
amounted to $66.4 million and $65.6 million, respectively (excluding school funds).  Additionally, the City 
received $129.2 million and $125.9 million for school operating purposes in fiscal years 2019 and 2018, 
respectively. 
Federal Aid 
State Aid discussed above includes $10.8 million in  federal pass-thru aid received by the City’s 
General Fund in fiscal year 2019, which was for public assistance, Healthy Families programs and indirect 
cost reimbursement from the Refuse Steam Plant.  St ate funding for public schools include $.9 million in 
federal funding. The City also received State and fede ral Funds in the form of specific grants which are 
accounted for in other Special Revenue Funds.  In fiscal year 2019 the City received $15.0 million in 
specific grant funding versus $15.8 million in fiscal year 2018.

General Fund  expenditures include, among other thi ngs, the cost of general government, transfer 
to the Debt Service Fund, transfer to the Capital Projects Fund, the City’s share of public school operating 
costs, public safety, highways and streets, culture and recreation, health and human services.  As shown in 
the following table, the significant areas of Genera l Fund expenditures are general government public 
safety, education and transfers to other funds.

Financial Operations: Preliminary (Unaudited) Fiscal Year 2020 Performance. General Fund 
revenue collections as of June 30, 2020 totaled $3 48.2 million, which is $1.9 million over budget ($4.8 
million over budget when you exclude  the budget underperformance fo r pass through revenues for the 
Community Development Agencies and other entities) and is $6.8 million over fiscal year 2019.  General 
Fund property tax collections, which make up approxima tely 50% of total estimated revenues, increased 
$5.3 million over fiscal year 2019. Other local taxes increased $0.9 million over fiscal year 2019.  General 
Fund expenditures as of June 30, 2020 totaled $339.6 million, which is $15.5 million under budget and $0.9 
million over fiscal year 2019.  The City anticipates an estimated fiscal year end 2020 surplus of $13 million, 
$5 million of  which will be  used to make a contribution to the City’s fiscal year 2021 capital budget and 
provide additional funding for the City’s Equipment Re placement Fund, leaving $8 million of the surplus 
to be added to the City’s unassigned fund balance. 
General Fund Budgetary Comparison Fiscal Year 2020 and Fiscal Year 2021 .  The General 
Fund fiscal year 2021 adopted budget totaling $516.9 m illion, represents a $29 million or 5.88% increase 
from the fiscal year 2020 adopted budget totaling $488.2 million.  General property tax revenues increased 
$6.5 million and other local revenues increased $4.8 m illion.  State revenues for the City public schools 
increased $16.6 million, increasing the City’s local cont ribution to public schools by $1.4 million.  The 
increase is based on the agreed upon school funding formula in which the school system receives a 
predetermined percentage of the growth in residential general property and utility taxes.  In addition to what 
is due the School system from the funding formula, th e City is also providing $380,000 in one-time funds 
to support the Career Academies. Retirement and employee benefits expenditures increased $6.7 million 
related to a 3% general wage increase along with a 2%  increase in the Virginia Retirement System (VRS) 
rate, and an increase in health insurance premiums.  Quality Government increased $1.6 million related to 
pay adjustments within this business team.  Contributions to outside agencies increased $0.6 million related 
to an increase in the per diem for community service agencies.

from the increase in personal service co sts (salaries, wages, fringe benefits) and the City’s share of public 
school operating costs.  Transfers to other funds have  increased primarily from an increase in debt service 
related to recent bond issues and the funding of various capital projects.
