Ladies and Gentlemen: 
 
We have examined the applicable law and ce rtified copies of pr oceedings and other 
documents relating to the issuance and sale by the City of Hampton, Virginia (the “City”) of its 
$10,725,000 General Obligation Public Improvemen t Bonds (Environmental Impact Bonds) 
(Green Bonds), Series 2020A (Tax-Exemp t) and $16,895,000 General Obligation Public 
Improvement Refunding Bonds, Seri es 2020B (Federally Taxable) (collectively, the “Bonds”).  
The Series 2020A Bonds are being issued to fund certain stormwater management projects and 
pay certain costs of issuing the Series 2020A Bonds. The Series 2020B Bonds are being issued to 
refund certain of the City’s outstanding Genera l Obligation Public Improvement Bonds, Series 
2013 and pay certain costs of issuing the Series 2020B Bonds.  Reference is made to the forms of 
the Bonds for information concerning their details, including payment and redemption provisions, 
and the proceedings pursuant to which they are issued. 
 Without undertaking to verify the same by independent investigation, we have also relied 
on (a) computations provided to Bingham Arbitrag e Rebate Services, Inc. the mathematical 
accuracy of which has been verified by them, rela ting to the yield of inve stments in the escrow 
fund established in connection with the refunding of the Refunded Bonds, the sufficiency of such 
investments to pay when due the principal of  and interest on the Refunded Bonds and (b)
 
certifications by representatives of the City and other public officials furnished to us as to certain 
facts relevant to both our opini on and requirements of the Inte rnal Revenue Code of 1986, as 
amended (the “Code”), including (i) the proceedi ngs authorizing the issuance of the Bonds and 
Refunded Bonds, (ii) the use, inve stment and expenditure of the pr oceeds of the  Bonds and the 
B-1 
 
Mayor and City Council 
City of Hampton 
December __, 2020 
Page 2 
 
Refunded Bonds and (iii) the projects financed or refinanced by the proceeds of the Bonds and the 
Refunded Bonds.   
 
 Based on the foregoing, it is our opinion that: 
 
(1) The Bonds have been authorized and i ssued in accordance with the Constitution 
and statutes of the Co mmonwealth of Virginia , including the Public Finance Act of 1991, and 
constitute valid and legally binding obligations of  the City, enforceable in  accordance with their 
terms, to which the faith and credit of the City are irrevocably pledged for the payment of principal 
thereof and interest thereon.  The Council of the City is authorized and re quired by law, unless 
other funds are lawfully available and appropriated for timely payment of the Bonds, to levy and 
collect annually, at the same time and in the same manner as other taxes of the City are assessed, 
levied and collected, ad valorem taxes on all locally taxable property in the City, without limitation 
as to rate or amount, over and above other taxes authorized or limited by law, sufficient to pay 
when due the principal of and interest on the Bonds. 
 
(2) Interest on the Series 2020A Bonds (including any original issue discount properly 
allocable to an owner thereof) is excludable fro m gross income for federal income tax purposes 
and is not an item of tax preference for purposes of the federal alternative minimum tax.  The 
opinion set forth in this paragra ph is subject to the condition th at the City co mply with all 
requirements of the Internal Revenue Code of 1986, as amended, that must be satisfied subsequent 
to the issuance of the Series 2020A Bonds in order that interest thereon be, or continue to be, 
excludable from gross income for federal income tax purposes.  The City has covenanted to comply 
with all such requirements.  Failure to comply with certain of such requirements may cause interest 
on the Series 2020A Bonds to be included in gr oss income for federa l income tax purposes 
retroactively to their date of issuance. 
 
(3) Interest on the Series 2020B Bonds is included in gross income for federal income 
tax purposes. 
 
(4) Interest on the Bonds is exempt from income taxation by the Commonwealth of 
Virginia. 
 
 The rights of the owners of the Bonds and th e enforceability of such rights may be limited 
or otherwise affected by bankruptcy, insolven cy, reorganization, moratorium and other laws 
affecting the rights of creditors generally and principles of equity, whether considered at law or in 
equity. 
 
Our services as bond counsel to the City ha ve been limited to a review of the legal 
proceedings required for the authorization of th e Bonds and to rendering the opinions set forth 
above.  We express no opinion herein as to the accuracy, adequacy or completeness of the Official 
Statement dated November 12, 2020 relating to the Bonds.  Further, we express no opinion 
B-2

regarding tax consequences arisi ng with respect to the Bonds othe r than as expressly set forth 
herein.   
Our opinion is based upon statutes, regulations, rulings and court decisions in effect on the 
date hereof. We undertake no obligation to update this opinion to reflect any facts or circumstances 
that may hereafter come to our attention or any changes in law that may hereafter occur.

APPENDIX C 
CONTINUING DISCLOSURE UNDERTAKING 
Pursuant to the Resolution, the City of Hampton, Virginia (the “City”), in connection with the issuance by 
the City of its $10,725,000 General Obligation Public Improvement Bonds (Environmental Impact Bonds) (Green 
Bonds), Series 2020A (Tax-Exempt) and its $16,895,000 General Obligation Public Improvement Refunding Bonds, 
Series 2020B (Federally Taxable) (collectively, the “Bonds”), the City has undertaken to comply with the provisions 
of Section (b)(5)(i) of Rule 15c2-12 (the “Rule”), prom ulgated by the Securities and Exchange Commission (the 
“SEC”) under the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, as th e same may be amended from  time to time, by providing 
certain annual financial information and event notices required by the Rule as follows: 
Section 1.  Continuing Disclosure .  (a) The City shall annually provide certain financial information and 
operating data in accordance with the provisions of Section (b)(5)(i) of the Rule, as follows: 
(i) Audited financial statements, prepared in  accordance with generally accepted accounting 
principles (GAAP) will be provided; and 
(ii) The operating data with respect to the City of the type appearing in the Official Statement 
in final form under the headings in Part II thereof en titled “Authorization of Debt,” “General Obligation Bonded 
Debt,” “Certain Debt Ratios,” and under the headings in Part III thereof entitled  “General Governmental Revenues” 
and “General Governmental Expenditures.” 
(b) The City shall annually provide the financial information and operating data described in subsection 
(a) above (the “Continuing Disclosure”) within 180 days after the end of the City’s fiscal year, commencing with its 
fiscal year ending June 30, 2020, to the Municipal Securities Rulemaking Board (the “MSRB”) utilizing its Electronic 
Municipal Market Access (“EMMA”) System or such other system designated by the MSRB. 
(c) Any of the Continuing Disclosure may be included by specific reference to other documents 
previously provided to the MSRB or filed with the SEC; provided, however, that any final official statement 
incorporated by reference must be available from the MSRB. 
(d) The City shall provide in a timely manner to the MSRB notice specifying any failure of the City to 
provide the Continuing Disclosure by the date specified. 
(e) If the City fails to comply with any covenant or  obligation specified in this section, any holder 
(within the meaning of the Rule) of Bonds then outstanding may, by notice to the City, proceed to protect and enforce 
its rights and the rights of the holders by an action for specific performance of the City’s covenant to provide financial 
information and operating data. 
Section 2.  Event Disclosure.  The City shall provide in a timely manner not in excess of ten (10) business 
days after the occurrence of the event to the MSRB through the EMMA system notice of the occurrence of any of the 
following events with respect to the Bonds: 
  (a)  principal and interest payment delinquencies; 
  (b) non-payment related defaults, if material; 
  (c) unscheduled draws on debt service reserves reflecting financial difficulties; 
  (d)  unscheduled draws on credit enhancement reflecting financial difficulties; 
  (e) substitution of credit or liquidity pr oviders, or their failure to perform;  
 
 
 
C-2 
  (f)  adverse tax opinions, the issuance by the Internal Revenue Service of proposed or 
final determinations of taxability, Notices of Proposed Issue (IRS Form 5701-TEB) or other material notices 
or determinations with respect to the tax status of the Bonds, or other material events affecting the tax status 
of the Bonds; 
  (g)  modifications to rights of Bond holders, if material; 
  (h)  bond calls, if material, and tender offers; 
  (i)  defeasances; 
  (j)  release, substitution, or sale of property securing repayment of the Bonds, if 
material;  
  (k)  rating changes; 
  (l) bankruptcy, insolvency, receive rship or similar event of the City; 
  Note to paragraph (l):  For the purposes of the event identified in paragraph (l), the event 
is considered to occur when any of the following occur:  The appointment of a receiver, fiscal agent or similar 
officer for an obligated person in proceeding under the U.S. Bankruptcy Code or in any other proceeding 
under state or federal law in which a court or governmental authority has assumed jurisdiction over 
substantially all of the assets or business of the obligated person, or if such jurisdiction has been assumed by 
leaving the existing governing body and officials or officers in possession but subject to the supervision and 
orders of a court or governmental authority, or the entry of an order confirming a plan of reorganization, 
arrangement or liquidation by a court or governmental authority having supervision or jurisdiction over 
substantially all of the assets or business of the obligated person. 
  (m) the consummation of a merger, consolidation or acquisition involving the City or 
sale of all or substantially all of the assets of the City, other than in the ordinary course of business, the entry 
into a definitive agreement to undertake such an action or the termination of a definitive agreement relating 
to any such actions, other than pursuant to its terms, if material;  
  (n) appointment of a successor or additional trustee or the change of name of a trustee, 
if material; 
  (o) incurrence of a financial obligation of the City, if material, or agreement to 
covenants, events of default, remedies, priority rights, or other similar terms of a financial obligation of the 
City, any of which affect security holders, if material; and 
  (p) default, event of acceleration, termina tion event, modification of terms, or other 
similar events under the terms of the financial obligation of the City, any of which reflect financial 
difficulties. 
The term “financial obligation” means a (i) debt oblig ation; (ii) derivative instrument entered into in 
connection with, or pledged as security or source of payment for, an existing or planned debt obligation; or 
(iii) guarantee of (i) or (ii). The term “financial obligation” shall not in clude municipal securities as to which a final 
official statement has been provided to the MSRB consistent with the Rule.
 
Section 3.  Termination .  The covenants and obligations of the City specified in Sections 1 and 2 will 
terminate upon the redemption, defeasance (within the meaning of the Rule) or payment in full of all the Bonds. 
Section 4.  Amendment.  The City reserves the right to modify its obligations contained in Sections 1 and 2 
without the consent of bondholders, provided that such modification complies with the Rule as it exists at the time of  
 
 
 
C-3 
modification.  The City shall within a reasonable tim e thereafter send to the MSRB a description of such 
modification(s). 
Section 5.  Additional Disclosure.  The City may from time to time disclose certain information and data in 
addition to the Continuing Disclosure.  Notwithstanding anything herein to the contrary, the City shall not incur any 
obligation to continue to provide, or to update, such additional information or data. 
Section 6.  Defaults. 
(a)  If the City fails to comply with any covena nt or obligation regarding Continuing Disclosure 
specified in this Disclosure Agreement, any holder (within the meaning of the Rule) of Bonds then outstanding may, 
by notice to the City, proceed to protect  and enforce its rights and the rights of  the holders by an action for specific 
performance of the City’s covenant to provide the Continuing Disclosure. 
(b)  Notwithstanding anything herein to the contrary, any failure of the City to comply with any 
obligation regarding Continuing Disclosure specified in this Disclosure Agreement (i) shall not be deemed to 
constitute an event of default under the Bonds or the resolution providing for the issuance of the Bonds and (ii) shall 
not give rise to any right or remedy other than that described in Section 6(a) above.

APPENDIX D 
BOOK-ENTRY ONLY 
The Depository Trust Company (“DTC”), Ne w York, New York, will act as securities 
depository for the Bonds.  The Bonds will be issued as fully-registered bonds and notes registered in the 
name of Cede & Co. (DTC’s partnership nominee) or such other name as may be requested by an authorized 
representative of DTC.  One fully-registered certificate for the Bonds will be issu ed for each maturity as 
set forth on the cover page of this Official Statem ent, each in the aggregate principal amount of such 
maturity, and will be deposited with DTC. 
DTC, the world’s largest depository, is a limited-purpose trust company organized under 
the New York Banking Law, a “banking organization” within the meaning of the New York Banking Law, 
a member of the Federal Reserve System, a “clearing corporation” within the meaning of the New York 
Uniform Commercial Code, and a “clearing agency” registered pursuant to the provisions of Section 17A 
of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934.  DTC holds and provides asset servicing for over 3.5 million issues 
of U.S. and non-U.S. equity issues, corporate and municipal debt issues, and money market instruments 
(from over 100 countries) that DTC’s participants (“Dir ect Participants”) deposit with DTC.  DTC also 
facilitates the post-trade-settlement among Direct Par ticipants of sales and other securities transactions in 
deposited securities, through electronic computerized book-entry transfers and pledges between Direct 
Participants’ accounts.  This eliminates the need for physical movement of securities certificates.  Direct 
Participants include both U.S. and non-U.S. securities brokers and dealers, banks, trust companies, clearing 
corporations, and certain other organizations.  DTC is a wholly-owned subsidiary of The Depository Trust 
& Clearing Corporation (“DTCC”).  DTCC is the holding company for DTC National Securities Clearing 
Corporation and Fixed Income Clearing Corporation, all of which are registered clearing agencies.  DTCC 
is owned by the users of its regulated subsidiaries. A ccess to the DTC system is also available to others 
such as both U.S. and non-U.S. securities brokers a nd dealers, banks, trust companies, and clearing 
corporations that clear through or maintain a custodial relationship with a Direct Participant, either directly 
or indirectly (“Indirect Participants”).  DTC has a Standard & Poor’s rating of AA+.  The DTC rules 
applicable to its Participants are on file with the Securities and Exchange Commission.  More information 
about DTC can be found at www.dtcc.com. 
Purchases of the Bonds under the DTC system must be made by or through Direct 
Participants, which will receive a credit for the Bond s on DTC’s records.  The ownership interest of each 
actual purchaser of each Bond (“Beneficial Owner”) is in  turn to be recorded on the Direct and Indirect 
Participants’ records.  Beneficial Owners will not receive written confirmation from DTC of their purchase.  
Beneficial Owners are, however, expected to r eceive written confirmations providing details of the 
transaction, as well as periodic statements of their holdings, from the Direct or Indirect Participant through 
which the Beneficial Owner entered into the transaction.  Transfers of ownership interests in the Bonds are 
to be accomplished by entries made on the books of Di rect and Indirect Particip ants acting on behalf of 
Beneficial Owners.  Beneficial Owners will not rece ive certificates representing their ownership interests 
in the Bonds, except in the event that use of the book-entry system for the Bonds is discontinued. 
To facilitate subsequent transfers, all Bonds deposited by Direct Participants with DTC are 
registered in the name of DTC’s partnership nominee, Cede & Co., or such other name as may be requested 
by an authorized representative of DTC.  The deposit of Bonds with DTC and their registration in the name 
of Cede & Co. or such other DTC nominee do not effect any change in beneficial ownership.  DTC has no 
knowledge of the actual Beneficial Owners of the B onds; DTC’s records reflect only the identity of the 
Direct Participants to whose accounts such Bonds ar e credited, which may or may not be the Beneficial  
 
 
 
D-2 
Owners.  The Direct and Indirect Participants will remain responsible for keeping account of their holdings 
on behalf of their customers. 
Conveyance of notices and other communications by DTC to Direct Participants, by Direct 
Participants to Indirect Participants, and by Direct Participants and Indirect Participants to Beneficial 
Owners will be governed by arrangements among them, subject to any statutory or regulatory requirements 
as may be in effect from time to time. 
Redemption notices shall be sent to DTC.  If less than all of the Bonds within an issue are 
being redeemed, DTC’s practice is to determine by lot the amount of the interest of each Direct Participant 
in such issue to be redeemed. 
Neither DTC nor Cede & Co. (nor any othe r DTC nominee) will consent or vote with 
respect to the Bonds unless authorized by a Direct Participant in accordance with DTC’s MMI Procedures.  
Under its usual procedures, DTC mails an Omnibus Proxy to issuers of obligations such as the Bonds as 
soon as possible after the record date.  The Omni bus Proxy assigns Cede & Co.’s consenting or voting 
rights to those Direct Participants to whose accounts th e Bonds are credited  on the record date (identified 
in a listing attached to the Omnibus Proxy). 
Principal and interest payments on the Bonds will be made to Cede & Co., or such other 
nominee as may be requested by an authorized representa tive of DTC.  DTC’s practice is to credit Direct 
Participants’ accounts upon DTC’s receipt of funds and corresponding detail information from the City, on 
payable date in accordance with their respective holdi ngs shown on DTC’s records.  Payments by Direct 
and Indirect Participants to Beneficial Owners will be governed by standing instructions and customary 
practices, as is the case with securities held for the account s of customers in bearer form or registered in 
“street name,” and will be the responsibility of such Di rect or Indirect Participant and not of DTC or the 
City, subject to any statutory or regulatory requirements as may be in effect from time to time.  Payment of 
principal and interest to Cede & Co. (or such other nominee as may be requested by an authorized 
representative of DTC) is the responsibility of the City, disbursement of such payments to Direct 
Participants will be the responsibility of DTC, and disbursement of such payments to the Beneficial Owners 
will be the responsibility of Direct and Indirect Participants. 
DTC may discontinue providing its services as securities depository with respect to the 
Bonds at any time by giving reasonable notice to the C ity.  Under such circumstan ces, in the event that a 
successor securities depository is not obtained, Bond certificates are required to be printed and delivered. 
The City may decide to discontinue use of the system of book-entry transfers through DTC 
(or a successor securities depository).  In such event, Bond certificates will be printed and delivered. 
The information in this section concerning DTC and DTC’s book-entry system has been obtained 
from sources that the City believes to be reliable, but the City takes no resp onsibility for the accuracy 
thereof.

Definitions 
The following definitions are used in this Technical Memorandum: 
 
- “City”: City Manager and City  Council of Hampton, VA. 
- “Contractor”: Firm contracted by the City to construct a portion of the Resiliency 
Projects.  
- “City of Hampton EIB”: The City  of Hampton General Obligation Public 
Improvement Bonds (Environmental Impact Bonds) (Green Bonds) Series 2020 
- “Environmental Impact Bond”: A debt instrument characterized by an evaluation 
and disclosure of project outcomes, as well as, in some cases, performance 
payments contingent on the measured outcomes being above or below pre-
determined thresholds. (Note that the City of Hampton EIB contains the 
evaluation and disclosure of project outcomes only, and not the performance 
payment feature.) 
- “Environmental, Social, and Co rporate Governance” (ESG): Non-financial 
characteristics of an organization or project that may inform financial or 
investment analysis. Examples include contributions to climate change 
adaptation, or addressing social or economic injustice.  
- “Final Report”: A report del ivered by the Validator to the City at the end of the 
measurement period that contains the validated measurement of the Primary 
Outcome Metric and its deviation from pre-construction estimates. 
- “International Capital Marke t Association Green Bond Principles”: Voluntary 
guidelines promulgated by the International Capital Market Association to 
promote standardization, transparency, and integrity in green bond issuances.  
- “Outcomes”: The various antic ipated impacts of the Resiliency Projects, such as 
flood mitigation, community green space, and other resiliency benefits. The 
Outcomes are discussed in more detail in Section 4 (“Discussion of Project 
Objectives”). 
- “Primary Outcome Metric”: T he project outcome chosen for measurement as part 
of the EIB transaction. In the case of the City of Hampton EIB, the Primary 
Outcome Metric is Gallons of Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity, aggregated 
across all three Projects. 
- “Purchaser(s)”: Investor(s),  Lender(s), or Institution(s) who purchase the City of 
Hampton EIB from the City.  
- “Resilient Infrastructure”: Res ilient infrastructure is planned, designed, and built 
specifically to both better withstand and mitigate the risks of climate-related 
disruptions and damages.1 Proceeds of this transaction will be used to construct 
the following types of project elements: 
o Bioswales and stormwat er retention terraces 
o In-stream storage accomplished b y stream widening and use of weirs to 
hold water further upstream 
o Road raising with associated median and roadside bioretention cells 
- “Resiliency Projects”: The th ree Resilient Infrastructure projects to be funded with 
the proceeds of the City of Hampton EIB. The Resiliency Projects are part of the 
 
1 http://www.oecd.org/environment/cc/policy-perspectives-climate-resilient-infrastructure.pdf                
 
5 
 
City’s Newmarket Creek Water Plan. They are discussed in more detail in 
Section 2 (“Description of Projects”). 
- “Stormwater Volume Storage Ca pacity”: The maximum volume of stormwater 
that the Resiliency Projects can store at a single point in time. Note that as a 
measurement of capacity, it represents a proxy measurement of the Resiliency 
Projects’ contribution to the City’s improved resilience profile rather than a 
prediction of the cumulative amount of water infiltrated by the Projects in any 
given year.  
- “United Nations Sustainable De velopment Goals”: A set of 17 ESG goals 
articulated by the United Nations that address shared global challenges and form 
“the blueprint to achieve a better and more sustainable future for all.”2 
- “Validator”: Third-party indepen dent entity that will inspect each project site and 
perform spot check measurements.    
 
2 https://www.un.org/sustainabledevelopment/sustainable-development-goals/                
 
6 
 
1. Introduction 
1.1 Purpose of the Technical Memorandum 
The purpose of this document is to describe the Resiliency Projects financed by the City 
of Hampton EIB, their goals and anticipated Outcomes, and the methodology behind 
pre-construction estimation and post-construction evaluation of the Primary Outcome 
Metric. It also discusses the selection process for the Resiliency Projects, how the bond 
proceeds will be limited to the Resiliency Projects, the qualifications of the City of 
Hampton EIB under certain current third-party evaluation frameworks, and the 
disclosure of Outcomes to the bondholders. 
 
1.2 Environmental Impact Bond Overview 
Environmental Impact Bonds, or EIBs, are a type of bond issuance that is characterized 
by two key features:  
 
1. Use of proceeds for environmentally and socially beneficial projects: Projects 
chosen to be funded by Environmental Impact Bonds have environmental and 
social benefits which can be articulated and quantified. The projects are 
consistent with well-known “green bond” guidelines such as the “Green Bond 
Principles” promulgated by the International Capital Market Association 
(“ICMA”) in June 2018.
3 The Resiliency Projects are nature-based climate 
change adaptation projects with stormwater management, flood mitigation, 
water quality improvement, and transportation resilience benefits. Eventually 
they will also include additional community co-benefits such as recreation.  
 
2. Outcome evaluation and disclosure: Each Environmental Impact Bond offering 
specifies one or more measurable project outcomes that will be quantified and 
disclosed to investors. The outcome(s) measured have a link to the projects’ 
overall environmental and/or social goals. For the City of Hampton EIB, the 
Primary Outcome Metric is gallons of stormwater volume storage capacity 
across the three Resiliency Projects.  
 
Some EIBs may include a performance payment feature, which allows the issuer to 
share risks and/or benefits of the projects with investors. Those payments are triggered 
when project outcomes are measured above or below pre-determined thresholds. 
These payments are sized to the projects’ costs and benefits based on an economic 
analysis. The City of Hampton EIB does not contain any performance payment features.  
 
The City of Hampton EIB will finance the construction of three Resiliency Projects in the 
Newmarket Creek watershed in Hampton, VA that provide resilience and community 
benefits to the City and surrounding neighborhoods by enhancing stormwater retention 
capacity and mitigating the effects of flooding relating to rainfall events and storm 
surges. As prototype projects, the City is interested in the evaluation of the resilience 
 
3 https://www.icmagroup.org/assets/documents/Regulatory/Green-Bonds/Green-Bonds-Principles-June-2018-270520.pdf                 
 
7 
 
outcomes that these capital projects provide in order to inform future investments in 
resiliency projects. 
 
1.3 Resilient Hampton and Newmarket Creek Water Plan Overview  
Resilient Hampton is a city-wide initiative across a number of City departments, 
including Community Development and Public Works, to coordinate a comprehensive 
effort to manage the City’s chronic and extreme water challenges from stormwater, 
tidally-induced, and storm surge flooding.  
 
Under its motto of “Living With Water”, the Resilient Hampton initiative embraces the 
City’s relationship with water as a historic waterfront community and harbor while 
bringing a holistic forward-thinking toolkit of flood risk mitigation, urban design, 
environmental restoration, community assets, and economic development.  
 
Phase I of the Resilient Hampton initiative began in 2015 with the five-day “Dutch 
Dialogues Virginia: Life at Sea Level” workshop organized and facilitated by experts 
from landscape architecture firm Waggonner & Ball and the Royal Netherlands 
Embassy. The City also collaborated with Joint Base Langley-Eustis on a Joint Land 
Use Study to assess varying hazard exposures, including flooding and climate change.  
 
The conclusion of Phase I, a report titled Living with Water Hampton: A Holistic 
Approach to Addressing Sea Level Rise and Resiliency, laid out a set of community-
based guiding principles to address resiliency needs. These principles are intended to 
address present-day challenges, taking into consideration the best available data 
related to sea level rise and climate change. The report identified significant water-
related challenges to the lives and property of area residents, including: increased 
instances of tidal flooding, also known as “sunny day flooding,” that affect residents’ 
ability to access both employment and critical services; larger and more frequent storm 
surge during extreme events such as hurricanes and nor’easters that can cause 
extensive property damage; patterns of repetitive property loss in affected areas; 
increases in flood insurance rates combined with decreasing property values; and the 
need to secure the area’s critical assets and industries, including national defense 
installations.
4 The guiding principles for the City’s response are that resiliency work in 
Hampton should be: Safe; Equitable; Natural; Heritage-preserving; Integrated; 
Sufficient; Nimble; and Innovative. 
 
As part of the ongoing Phase II, the City is implementing these community-based 
principles in a pilot area: the Newmarket Creek watershed. The Newmarket Creek 
Water Plan was developed after nearly two years of expert consultation, design work, 
public engagement, and planning. Community feedback was an integral part throughout 
the Plan development, including through two public forums in January 2019 and 
January 2020 where the vision and projects were presented for public comments. The 
Plan lays out not only a high-level strategic vision of the watershed’s strategic priorities, 
 
4 Waggonner & Ball, “Living with Water Hampton: A Holistic Approach to Addressing Sea Level Rise and Resiliency” (2017), at p. 
18, https://hampton.gov/DocumentCenter/View/20644/Resilient-Ham pton-Phase-I-Report?bidId=.                
 
8 
 
but also specific projects which are themselves replicable pilots for successive 
generations of resiliency work in Hampton.  
 
In addition, the Plan set a goal of using non-traditional forms of financing such as an 
Environmental Impact Bond to finance these projects. The City intends to use the City of 
Hampton EIB and its outcomes evaluation features to aid in future capital planning 
decisions, measure benefits to multiple stakeholders, and publicize the benefits of City 
projects to the communities they serve. 
2. Description of Resiliency Projects 
2.1 Description of Project Area and Needs: Newmarket Creek Watershed 
Newmarket Creek is central to the City of Hampton’s water management challenges 
that are causing more frequent and severe flooding than in the past. These challenges 
come from a variety of sources: precipitation, tidal forces, sea level rise, subsidence of 
an already low elevation, increased impervious surface, and depleted groundwater 
reservoirs.  
 
 
Figure 1: Newmarket Creek watershed and the City of Hampton municipal boundaries  
Credit: Waggonner & Ball 
               
 
9 
 
 
The Creek runs through key parts of the City until it meets the Back River estuary of the 
Chesapeake Bay near the city center. It collects and conveys stormwater from its 
watershed, which encompasses a large part of the City, including residential 
neighborhoods, commercial districts, and municipal campuses. (Figure 1) 
 
Stormwater management is a challenge for the City for a few different reasons. 
Impervious surfaces in the Creek watershed have increased 19% since 20005, while the 
generally high groundwater table has limited capacity for infiltration into the soil. In 
addition, when the rain-laden Creek meets the Bay, tidal forces can push water back up 
the Creek, causing flooding throughout the watershed.  
 
 
Figure 2: Repetitive loss flooding areas and storm surge in the Newmarket Creek watershed  
Credit: Waggonner & Ball 
 
The City is anticipating water management to be a growing concern in the future. 
Climate change is and will continue to cause more intense and frequent rainstorms. 
Areas of low elevation near the water are already being repeatedly flooded. (Figure 2) A 
large part of the City is just ten feet above sea level or less. In addition, the Hampton 
Roads region has one of the highest rates of land subsidence in the US, with large parts 
of the City of Hampton sinking at up to 2.4 mm / yr, and the highest rate of relative sea 
level rise on the Atlantic Coast.6   
 
5 https://www.dailypress.com/news/hampton/dp-nws-hampton-city-council-nasa-20190813-story.html 
6 https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-017-15309-5  
               
 
10 
 
 
Successful mitigation and adaptation to these future water challenges is critical to 
preserving the economic future and way of life in Hampton. The importance of this work 
is heightened in the areas of the City within the Newmarket Creek watershed, not only 
because of its proximity to the water, but also because of the prevailing socioeconomic 
conditions in those affected neighborhoods, as seen in the data below. (The 
background data and methodology are given in the Appendix.)

2.2 Process for Project Evaluation and Selection 
As part of the Resilient Hampton initiative, the City conducted a multi-year process to 
identify resiliency challenges and opportunities. Beginning in 2015, the City engaged in 
the “Dutch Dialogues Virginia,” a workshop that brought together urban water 
management experts from New Orleans and the Netherlands — engineers, architects, 
and planners — to meet with their counterparts in Hampton Roads to discuss ideas and 
solutions for the future. After that meeting, the City pledged to be a leader in coastal 
resiliency. Since then, Hampton has engaged Waggoner & Ball Architects (a firm based 
in New Orleans who were lead participants in the Dutch Dialogues workshop) to help 
gather data and develop an integrated approach to coastal resiliency, based on the 
Dutch approach to life at sea level and the motto “Living with Water”. Rather than look 
at flooding alone, this approach helps integrate flood risk mitigation, engineering, urban 
design, environmental restoration, community assets, and economic development.7 
 
The City Council selected the Newmarket Creek watershed as the first pilot project 
area. In January 2019, Waggonner & Ball and their Dutch colleagues from 
BoschSlabbers convened in Hampton for a week-long design workshop that included 
stakeholders from a range of organizations including Old Dominion University, Hampton 
University, the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers, Newport News and Poquoson officials, 
the Hampton Roads Planning District Commission, Joint Base Langley–Eustis Air Force 
Base, NASA, the Virginia Institute of Marine Science (VIMS), the Virginia Marine 
Resources Commission (VMRC), and more. The purpose of this workshop was to 
develop project proposals for areas of the City where water can be managed before it 
reaches Newmarket Creek.  
 
One of the key principles was to slow, store, and redirect water to reduce flooding 
impacts in ways consistent with the Resilient Hampton principles of promoting 
sustainability and the protection and incorporation of natural resources into flooding 
responses. Projects were envisioned all along the creek and throughout the watershed. 
The conceptual designs prioritized during the workshop included the Resiliency Projects 
selected for funding through the City of Hampton EIB by the City’s Finance, Planning, 
and Public Works departments and approved by the City Council.
8 
 
The Resiliency Projects were chosen to be funded by the EIB due to their contribution 
towards addressing the needs of the Newmarket Creek Watershed and improving the 
City’s resiliency profile under the Resilient Hampton initiative. Their primary impact is 
measured by their capacity for storing stormwater, which is a major factor in both 
chronic and extreme flooding. In addition, the Resiliency Projects will be assessed 
based on their ability to improve living conditions and reduce flooding in low-income 
neighborhoods in the affected watershed. (See Section 4.1.) Both are aligned with well-
known green bond and ESG frameworks, as discussed in Section 4.2. The City will fulfill 
these Resiliency Projects’ status as prototypes by collecting data about their realized 
 
7 https://hampton.gov/3466/Dutch-Dialogues 
8 https://hampton.gov/3599/Phase-II-and-Pilot-Area                
 
13 
 
impacts through the EIB outcome evaluation protocol, which will improve decision-
making and capital planning for future resiliency efforts. 
 
2.3 Project Description: Big Bethel Blueway 
Big Bethel Blueway is a green infrastructure project that will store and slow water 
through the redesign of existing waterways to reduce flooding upstream in residential 
neighborhoods, as well as downstream in Newmarket Creek. The installation of several 
weirs in the main drainage channel will create the ability to store stormwater where 
there previously was none. Expansion of the main channel and several of the smaller 
backyard drainage runnels that feed into it will increase their stormwater storage 
capacity. Installing vegetation on the banks will aid in the slowing, infiltration, and 
filtration of water, as well as creating visual privacy for adjacent residential parcels. The 
Blueway will have several distinctive segments along its length based on ecological, 
topographic, and urban typologies. As the Blueway approaches Newmarket Creek, 
ecological typologies will change to recreate ones found in coastal Virginia, where each 
has a different planting and stormwater strategy. Separate funding is anticipated to 
redesign the Blueway's existing maintenance path to become a recreational trail and to 
accommodate more stormwater storage.9  
 
2.4 Project Description: North Armistead Avenue Road Raising and Green Infrastructure 
North Armistead Avenue is a major route for traffic through the city and serves as a 
major connector of economic drivers like Joint Base Langley-Eustis, downtown 
Hampton, and the Coliseum Central district. In addition, North Armistead Avenue 
connects to key evacuation routes. Because the project area is low-lying, the roadway 
already sees frequent flood-related transportation disruptions, hampering reliable 
transportation to the military base and key economic centers. To mitigate this flood risk, 
a portion of North Armistead Avenue between Findlay Street and the bridge just west of 
Lasalle Avenue will be raised by several feet. In connection with the road raising, green 
infrastructure will be installed along the southbound lanes in the public right of way and 
in the medians to help slow, store, and redirect stormwater within the spaces adjacent 
to the elevated roadway. This system will be connected to the drainage improvements 
at Lake Hampton.10 Separate funding is anticipated to establish a shared use path along 
the road.  
 
2.5 Project Description: Lake Hampton 
Lake Hampton will be transformed from a retention pond into a stormwater park that 
increases the site’s existing stormwater storage capacity and yields multiple benefits for 
the community. Stormwater from the adjacent neighborhood to the northwest is 
currently conveyed to the lake in a re-routed drainage line. When the lake fills, it 
overtops its weir to drain into Newmarket Creek. The project will raise the height of the 
dam and weir and install a series of detention basins with wetland plantings in a 
 
9 Big Bethel Blueway project summary. 
10 North Armistead Avenue Green Infrastructure project summary.                
 
14 
 
“treatment train” to slow, store, and clean runoff from North Armistead Avenue before it 
enters the lake. Separate funding is anticipated to extend the existing Water Walk trail 
as a multi-use trail around the lake, bringing people alongside Newmarket Creek.11 
3. Allocation of City of Hampton EIB Proceeds 
3.1 Use of City of Hampton EIB Proceeds by Project 
The City of Hampton EIB is being issued to finance costs of the Resiliency Projects, 
which constitute three green infrastructure and flood mitigation projects identified and 
undertaken pursuant to the Resilient Hampton initiative. 12

3.2 Management of City of Hampton EIB Proceeds 
The net proceeds of the City of Hampton EIB remaining after deduction of issuance 
costs will be used by the City to pay costs of the Resiliency Projects. Such amounts will 
be tracked and disbursed for such purpose and, pending disbursement, will be invested 
in accordance with Virginia law. 
4. Discussion of Project Objectives 
4.1 Primary Project Objectives and Description of Impacts 
These Resiliency Projects are climate change adaptation and flood mitigation projects 
to address both chronic and extreme flooding in the Newmarket Creek watershed. 
Flooding in Hampton comes from two main sources: stormwater runoff during rain 
events, and tidal forces. Local studies indicate that the quantity of annual precipitation is 
already increasing in coastal Virginia. Severity of both chronic and extreme rain events 
are increasing as well: the one-year storm (which represents a storm moderate enough 
to be expected every year) is now expected to occur every 9 months13; the regional ten-
 
11 Lake Hampton project summary. 
12 https://hampton.gov/3459/Resilient-Hampton 
13 “Recurrent Flooding Study for Tidewater Virginia”, Virginia Institute of Marine Sciences 2013                
 
15 
 
year storm (which represents a storm unusual enough in its severity to only have a 10% 
chance of occurring in any given year) has increased its amount of rainfall compared to 
existing estimates by up to 10%.14 Other threats include flooding resulting from storm 
surges and long-term elevation and sea level changes.   
 
The Resiliency Projects will mitigate both chronic and extreme flooding by increasing 
the watershed’s capacity to capture and store runoff before it reaches Newmarket 
Creek. (See Section 5.) By improving stormwater retention and infiltration and reducing 
the amount of stormwater overflowing the Creek’s banks, the Resiliency Projects benefit 
not only the directly adjacent neighborhoods, but also the neighborhoods downstream. 
This accomplishes the Resiliency Projects’ design goals of slowing and storing water.  
 
In addition, the North Armistead Avenue road raising project is designed to address 
traffic disruptions due to chronic flooding on this critical transportation corridor. Raising 
the elevation of the road will greatly reduce its susceptibility to chronic tidal forces, as 
well as sea level rise due to climate change. Based on the current height of the road, 
water levels, and past traffic data, the road currently experiences an average of 92 
hours of flooding per year, affecting approximately 45,000 vehicles annually. Current 
projections indicate the Project will eliminate annual recurring flooding and the 
associated transportation disruptions. 
 
Year Total Hours of Flooding Total Number of Affected Cars

2017 84:02 42,845

Table 3: Traffic disruptions on North Armistead Avenue due to flooding in current conditions, 2015 - 2019 
 
In addition to the measured impact of stormwater volume storage capacity and traffic 
resilience, the Resiliency Projects have numerous other benefits. The installed 
vegetation will reduce urban heat island, improve air quality, and provide neighborhood 
green space.

5. Project Impact Estimation, as Designed Pre-Construction  
5.1 Primary Project Outcome Metric: Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity 
The primary Outcome Metric for the Resiliency Projects will be Stormwater Volume 
Storage Capacity (gallons). The aggregate volume storage capacity for the Resiliency 
Projects is estimated to be 8.62 million gallons. Although there are multiple ways to 
measure the resilience benefits of these Resiliency Projects, this metric was chosen 
because it is a meaningful proxy measure of the resilience benefits, decision-useful for 
the City in capital planning of future resiliency projects, and able to be measured cost-
efficiently (see Section 6, “Project Impact Evaluation, Post-Construction”): 
 
● Directly relevant to project goals: A significant amount of localized flooding in 
Hampton is driven by stormwater, making storage provided by the Resiliency 
Projects during rain events a key measure of flood resilience 
● Decision-useful: The Resiliency Projects are intended to be re plicable pilots that 
inform future capital planning decisions, making data about the cost-efficiency of 
localized flood mitigation projects valuable. 
● Objective: Measurements are tak en by registered land surveyors, verified by 
professional engineers, and can be repeated if desired. 
● Cost-effective to quantify: Vol ume storage capacity can be measured via one 
post-construction survey, and verified by a third party using spot check survey 
measurements.                
 
19 
 
 
As a capacity measure, the Resiliency Projects’ storage capacity represents the 
maximum amount of stormwater they can store every time it rains. This will especially 
benefit the City by absorbing chronic runoff during those less severe, more frequent rain 
events. In aggregate, the Resiliency Projects are expected to add 8.62 million gallons of 
stormwater volume storage capacity, or 26.4 acre-feet. One acre-foot is approximately 
equal to filling a football field with water to a height of one foot. The expected 
performance of the Resiliency Projects is given in the table below. 
 
Resiliency Project Expected Added Stormwater Volume Storage

In addition, as described in Section 4.1, the road raising portion of the North Armistead 
Avenue Project is expected to eliminate the impacts of chronic flooding, which currently 
causes 92 hours of flooding and affects 45,000 vehicles in an average year.  
 
The flood resilience benefits can also be thought of in terms of more infrequent but 
more extreme rain events. A “ten-year storm” is a rain event unusual enough in its 
severity to only have a 10% chance of occurring in any given year. These storms are 
more likely to flood residential areas and disrupt economic activity. Capturing as much 
runoff as possible is a critical step to managing flooding during these extreme events, 
which have become more frequent in coastal Virginia in recent years.

A ten-year storm also creates about 207,000 gallons of runoff from the average 
commercial parcel in Hampton, and about 28,000 gallons of runoff from the average 
single-family residential parcel. With their combined storage capacity of 8.62 million 
gallons, the Resiliency Projects are projected to create stormwater volume capture 
equivalent to the runoff from 27 commercial parcels, or 200 single-family residential 
parcels, during a ten-year rain event. 
 
5.2 Overview of Pre-Construction Outcome Estimation Approach 
The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity achieved by the Resiliency Projects can be 
estimated using the engineering design drawings at the time of the issuance of the City 
of Hampton EIB. The storage capacity of each Resiliency Project is dependent on the 
geometric dimensions of several of its key design features. Using the dimensions in the 
design drawings allows pre-construction estimation of the Stormwater Volume Storage 
Capacity created by each Resiliency Project.  
 
Big Bethel Blueway: The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity added by this project 
comes from the addition of weirs and the expansion of the drainage channels. Since the 
existing channel does not have weirs, it does not currently have the ability to store 
stormwater. The weirs will hold back stormwater runoff collected from the nearby 
neighborhoods until it tops the weirs, reducing the rate of water flowing downstream into 
Newmarket Creek. The main drainage channel behind the weirs will be widened, 
increasing the amount of water that can be stored behind the weirs. Further, the 
backyard drainage swales that connect and contribute to the main drainage channel will 
have their storage capacity increased through the addition of bioretention cells. In the 
future, a site directly adjacent to the Blueway, currently a school site no longer in use, 
may receive green infrastructure installations to further enhance the stormwater 
management ability of the weirs.  
 
North Armistead Avenue Road Raising and Green Infrastructure: The Stormwater 
Volume Storage Capacity added by this project comes from the installation of bioswales 
in the median of the road and between the road and the shared-use path, which will 
hold back and infiltrate stormwater runoff from the road itself. The surface storage 
capacity in the median and roadside bioswales will be an increase over present 
conditions.  It is also likely that underground storage and infiltration capacity can be 
included as bioretention cells under the median and roadside bioswales. 
 
Lake Hampton: The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity added by this project comes 
from two key design features. First, the majority of the lake’s storage capacity comes 
from precipitation filling the lake, preventing water from entering Newmarket Creek until 
the lake’s levels top its weir into the Creek. The improvements made through the project 
to the outflow structures, including raising the height of the weir and the dam, will allow 
the lake to store an increased amount of precipitation before it tops the weir. Second, 
the project will add a number of detention basins in a “treatment train” that will capture                
 
21 
 
excess stormwater runoff from North Armistead Avenue. Each basin will fill in sequence, 
sending its excess volume to the basin adjacent to it, and eventually to the lake itself.  
 
5.3 Calculations of Pre-Construction Outcome and Results 
The following sections discuss the methodology used by the City’s engineering 
consultants, Moffatt & Nichol, to calculate the as-designed Stormwater Volume Storage 
Capacity of each Resiliency Projects. 
5.3.1. Big Bethel Blueway  
The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity created by this project is the sum of the 
storage capacity in the main drainage channel (the Blueway), plus the storage capacity 
in the bioretention cells to be created along several drainage easements running 
between residential properties adjacent to the channel. 
 
The estimated storage capacity in the channel is calculated over intervals of 100 feet 
along the stream, by multiplying the 100 feet length by the average cross-sectional 
storage area available at each end of that interval. This is an “average end area” 
calculation method that is a typical method used in engineering and construction for 
establishing quantities. The storage available at each cross-section is the area bounded 
by the stream channel topography (the expanded banks and stream bed) and below the 
next downstream weir elevation associated with each cross-section. The calculation for 
a typical 100-foot interval is illustrated in Figures 3 and 4 below. The storage capacity is 
the average of the storage area at the upstream cross-section (A1) and the downstream 
cross-section (A2) times the length between cross-sections (L), which is 100 feet, so 
that: Blueway in-stream storage capacity at each 100-foot interval = (A1+A2)/2 x L. 
                
 
22 
 
 
 
Figure 6: Big Bethel Blueway cross-section channel storage calculation example 
 
 
 
Figure 7: Big Bethel Blueway profile between example cross-sections 
 
At this example location between cross-section stations 15+00 and 16+00, about 500 
feet upstream of the proposed weir at Mercury Boulevard, the weir would retain water to 
an elevation of 11.5 feet NAVD88. The cross-section at 15+00 has a storage of 76.53 
cubic feet per foot along the stream (cf/ft), and the cross-section at 16+00 has a storage 
of 79.64 cf/ft. Taking the average of these, 78.09 cf/ft, and multiplying by 100 feet 
between the cross-sections gives a total storage of 7,809 cubic feet between these 
locations. Summing the storage at all of the 100-foot intervals in the Blueway results in 
               
 
23 
 
a total in-stream storage capacity of approximately 310,530 cubic feet, equivalent to 
approximately 2.32 million gallons. 
 
The storage capacity in the bioretention cells adjacent to the Blueway is a function of 
the length of the drainage easement available for constructing the bioretention cells and 
the cross-sectional area of the cells below existing grade. Since these easements are 
currently drained by a very shallow swale running along the existing grade, it is 
important that the bioretention cells not cause runoff to pond higher than those existing 
ground elevations. Thus, the storage created by the bioretention cells is all 
underground, within a layer of porous media (e.g. gravel or other similar granular 
material). This means that the storage capacity calculation includes the cross-sectional 
storage area, A, the length of the cells, L, and the void ratio between the porous media, 
R. The area is the Width, W, times the average Depth, D, and the actual calculation is:  
Bioretention cell storage capacity = W x D x L x R. Figure 5 below shows an example 
calculation for the bioretention cell between Sunnyside Drive and Weaver Road just 
south of Mercury Boulevard. Summing the storage at all of the nine bioretention cells 
adjacent to the Blueway results in a total storage capacity of approximately 50,662 
cubic feet, equivalent to approximately 0.38 million gallons. 
                
 
24 
 
 
 
Figure 8: Big Bethel Blueway drainage swale storage area example calculation 
 
5.3.2. North Armistead Avenue Road Raising and Green Infrastructure  
The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity created by this project is the sum of the 
capacities added by bioswales in the road median and on the roadside. The estimated 
added storage capacity in the median is calculated as the cross-sectional area of the 
median, shown shaded in blue in Figure 6 below, times the length of median associated 
with the raised roadway segment. The estimated added storage capacity created by the 
roadside bioswale is calculated similarly. The cross-section area for both the median 
and the bioswale may vary along the length of the roadway project, and the area is 
               
 
25 
 
calculated by the average end area method as described for the storage in the main 
channel of the Big Bethel Blueway, described above. 
 
 
 
Figure 9: North Armistead Ave road raising and stormwater volume capture typical section 
 
The project may ultimately include subsurface bioretention cells constructed below the 
median and/or the roadside bioswale. The purpose of the bioretention cells would be to 
provide additional stormwater volume capture and infiltration, below the grade for the 
median and bioswale allowed by current roadway design guidelines. The potential 
bioretention cells are illustrated in Figure 6 as green-shaded areas of approximately 1.5 
feet in depth below the median and roadside bioswale. Due to the uncertainty of 
including these in the ultimate project design, the volume of the potential additional 
subsurface bioretention cells has not been included in the storage capacity calculation 
at this time. 
 
5.3.3. Lake Hampton  
The Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity created by this project is the sum of the 
additional storage capacity created in the lake itself, plus the storage capacity created in 
the detention basins.   
 
The estimated added storage capacity in the lake is calculated as the footprint area of 
the lake at its normal daily high tide water level times the depth of water storage allowed 
by the raising of the dam and raising of the outflow weirs that control flow out to 
Newmarket Creek. The lake’s current normal high tide level is approximately elevation 
1.2 feet NAVD88, and the target storage elevation is 3.5 feet NAVD88. Figure 7 
illustrates the additional storage in the lake created by the project’s features. The 
effective footprint area of storage in the lake is approximately 304,000 square feet. 
Thus, the project would result in the lake increasing its total storage area of 
approximately 699,200 cubic feet, or 5.23 million gallons.  
 
               
 
26 
 
 
 
Figure 10: Additional storage capacity created by the Project in Lake Hampton above typical present level 
 
The estimated added storage capacity in the stormwater detention basins between the 
lake and the eastbound lanes of N. Armistead Ave. is calculated as the sum of the 
storage for each of the individual basins. This storage for each basin is its bottom 
footprint area times the difference between each basin’s overflow elevation (toward the 
next downstream basin) and its bed elevation. The footprint areas of storage vary by 
basin, and the storage depth in each basin is approximately 1.5 feet, as illustrated in 
Figure 8. The total storage achievable for the sum of all of the basins is approximately 
47,586 cubic feet, or 0.36 million gallons. 
 
               
 
27 
 
 
 
Figure 11: Storage area example for Lake Hampton stormwater detention basins 
6. Project Impact Evaluation, Post-Construction 
6.1 Approach to Third-Party Validated Outcome Evaluation 
The realized post-construction Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity may be different 
than originally designed due to a number of factors, including variability in cost of 
materials and labor, variability in Contractor bid prices, unforeseen utility lines, and 
available easements. The EIB Outcome Evaluation is intended to provide the City with 
direct feedback about the degree to which the Resiliency Projects provide the resiliency 
benefits as anticipated in engineering designs before bond issuance.  
 
               
 
28 
 
6.2 Qualifications of Third-Party Validator 
The City will contract with a Third-Party Validator once the Resiliency Projects are 
nearing completion and ready for outcome evaluation. To preserve objectivity, the Third-
Party Validator has not and will not have been involved in the City of Hampton EIB or its 
Resiliency Projects up until that point. The City has already prepared an RFP for the 
Third-Party Validator. (See Appendix A.1.) The RFP will be distributed to the several 
engineering firms with which the City has existing contracts for general engineering 
services, any of which meet the qualifications to be the EIB Third-Party Validator.  
 
6.3 Outcome Evaluation Methodology 
The measurement of as-built Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity will be evaluated in 
two steps. First, the Contractor will measure final volume storage capacity after 
completing construction of each project by conducting an as-built survey of each project 
site. The surveys will measure the geometric dimensions of each Resiliency Project that 
determine its Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity. The Contractor will submit a survey 
report to the City that includes the measurements of each Resiliency Project’s 
geometric dimensions.  
 
Second, the independent Third-Party Validator will conduct a site inspection of each 
project to corroborate the Contractor’s as-built survey, including spot check field 
measurements and photography. Once site inspections are complete at each Resiliency 
Project, the Validator will include the Contractor’s verified outcome report and a 
comparison of as-built Volume Storage Capacity to as-designed Volume Storage 
Capacity in a Final Report to be shared with the City.

7. Environmental Impact Bond and Green Bond Reporting 
The City expects to report annually on a voluntary basis on the expenditures of the 
proceeds of the Bonds and the construction progress of the Resiliency Projects through 
completion of all Resiliency Projects. This report will also identify the Resiliency 
Projects’ alignment with the U.N. Sustainable Development Goals. Following completion 
of the Resiliency Projects, the City plans to provide a one-time, voluntary disclosure 
detailing the measured and independently verified outcome metric described above, 
Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity. The City may modify, amend, or discontinue the 
voluntary disclosure at any time.  
 
7.1 Annual Disclosure Regarding Use of Bond Proceeds 
The City intends to post on EMMA and on the Resilient Hampton website 
(https://hampton.gov/3459/Resilient-Hampton) annually a description of the expenditure 
of the proceeds of the Bonds and the progress of the Resiliency Projects. This report 
will also identify the Resiliency Projects’ alignment with the U.N. Sustainable 
Development Goals.  
 
7.2 Project Completion Disclosure 
Within six (6) months of completion of each Resiliency Project and receipt of 
Independent Validator Final Report, the City intends to post on EMMA and on the 
Resilient Hampton website a report prepared by the Independent Validator to 
corroborate the construction and completion of the Resiliency Projects in accordance 
with their plans and specifications, and include in such report a verified comparison of 
Stormwater Volume Storage Capacity as-built to as-designed.                  
 
30 
 
Appendix 
Appendix A.1: City of Hampton Environmental Impact Bond – Independent Validator 
Scope of Services 
The proposed scope of services to provide an independent validation of the outcome 
metric outlined for the three Environmental Impact Bond Projects:  
Big Bethel Blueway is a green infrastructure project that will store and slow water 
through the redesign of existing waterways in order to reduce flooding upstream in 
residential neighborhoods, as well as downstream in Newmarket Creek. The installation 
of several weirs in the main drainage channel will create the ability to store stormwater 
where there previously was none. Expansion of the main channel and several of the 
smaller backyard drainage runnels that feed into it will increase their stormwater storage 
capacity. Installing vegetation on the banks will aid in the slowing, infiltration, and 
filtration of water, as well as creating visual privacy for adjacent residential parcels. The 
Blueway will have several distinctive segments along its length based on ecological, 
topographic, and urban typologies. As the Blueway approaches Newmarket Creek, 
ecological typologies will change to recreate ones found in coastal Virginia, where each 
has a different planting and stormwater strategy. Separate funding is anticipated to 
redesign the Blueway's existing maintenance path to become a recreational trail and to 
accommodate more stormwater storage. 
North Armistead Avenue is a major route for traffic through the city and serves as a 
major connector of economic drivers like Joint Base Langley-Eustis, downtown 
Hampton, and the Coliseum Central district. In addition, North Armistead Avenue 
connects to key evacuation routes. Because the project area is low-lying, the roadway 
already sees frequent flood-related transportation disruptions, hampering reliable 
transportation to the military base and key economic centers. To mitigate this flood risk, 
a portion of North Armistead Avenue between Findlay Street and the bridge just west of 
Lasalle Avenue will be raised by several feet. In connection with the road raising, green 
infrastructure will be installed to help slow, store, and redirect stormwater within the 
space adjacent to the elevated roadway. A bioswale along the southbound lanes in the 
public right of way and bioswales in the medians will be constructed above groundwater 
levels to provide water storage. This system will be connected to the drainage 
improvements at Lake Hampton. Separate funding is anticipated to establish a shared 
use path along the road. 
Lake Hampton will be transformed from a retention pond into a stormwater park that 
boosts the site’s existing stormwater storage capacity and yields multiple benefits for 
the community. Stormwater from the adjacent neighborhood to the northwest is 
currently conveyed to the lake in a re-routed drainage line; when the lake fills, it 
overtops its weir to drain into Newmarket Creek. The project will raise the height of the 
dam and weir and install a series of detention basins with wetland plantings in a 
“treatment train” to slow, store, and clean runoff from North Armistead Avenue before it                
 
31 
 
enters the lake. Separate funding is anticipated to extend the existing Water Walk trail 
as a multi-use trail around the lake, bringing people alongside Newmarket Creek. 
Performance metrics will include the volume of water storage capacity within the 
system. This storage will be utilized to detain, store and infiltrate runoff during storm 
events resulting in a reduction of nuisance flooding observed within the project areas. 
The following three tasks have been outlined to validate the volume of water storage 
attained through the construction of these projects and identify any discrepancies. 
 
Task 1 – Construction Phase Services 
The VALIDATOR shall perform an inspection approximately at the midpoint of 
construction of each project and report to the city any perceived challenges to achieving 
the stormwater volume storage capacity goals for the project as designed. 
Deliverables: The VALIDATOR will provide an inspection report for each project area, 
identifying their observations of the project progress, estimated percent complete. 
Identifying any perceived challenges to meeting the stated volume capture goals of 
each project. 
Schedule: The City will conduct site visits on a regular basis to monitor progress. The 
Validator will coordinate with the City and Contractor and conduct an inspection after 
construction has reached at least a fifty (50) percent completion. 
 
Task 2 – Post Construction 
The VALIDATOR will verify the Contractor’s as-built drawings provided by the City in 
Civil 3D format.  
 The verification will consist o f the VALIDATOR making site visits to each of the 
project sites and utilizing” good engineering practice and standards” to confirm 
the as-built survey matches the design drawings within approximately 5% error.  
 Spot check measurements to verif y the as-built survey, including photography, 
representative field survey measurements. 
 Calculate volume storage capacit y from the as-built measurements using Civil 
3D, using the methodology utilized by Moffatt & Nichol during the development of 
the design drawings. 
Deliverables: NONE  
Schedule: The Validator will begin Task 2 as soon as reasonably possible but no later 
than one (1) month after completion of the project construction.                
 
32 
 
 
Task 3 – Final Report 
In collaboration with City staff, the ENGINEER will incorporate proposed SWMFs into 
the existing conditions PCSWMM model to create the future conditions model.  Using 
the model, an iterative process will be implemented to size the SWMFs.  Prior to 
beginning the sizing process, the ENGINEER will collaborate with the City to determine 
the level of service (design criteria) for the proposed SWMFs. Information such as sizes, 
locations, and elevations of proposed diversion piping, along with stage/area data of 
proposed SWMFs, and outlet structure configurations, will be sized at a preliminary 
level. A summary of flows and peak WSEs will be summarized. 
Based on comments received during the future conditions review meeting (Task 1), the 
future conditions model will be finalized. 
Deliverables: The Validator will submit three (3) reports, one for each project. Each 
report shall include the following: 
 Assessment of the project site , identifying any significant deviations from the 
original approved design, status of vegetation at the time the report was written. 
Any observations regarding water levels and system functionality. 
 Calculation deviation of the m easured Volume Storage Capacity, if any, to 
Project Designs (via as-built Survey) 
 Conclusion as to whether the proj ect Outcomes meet the specific volume storage 
capacity for the specific project as outlined in the EIB Project Design 
Schedule: The Validator will complete the final task within 180 days after the 
submission of the Certificate of Completion from the City to the Validator. 
 
 
                 
 
33

calculated based on data from the U.S. Census Bureau 2014-2018 American 
Community Survey (ACS). Census tracts in Hampton are identified as State FIPS code

against the area median income of a household in the City as a whole, which was 
$54,550 (2018 USD).
